RATING: 9
O&L reminds me of Le Havre and Roads & Boats; it has a spatial component in terms of settlements (where as Le Havre only had the resource conversion) but it's abstracted away most of the logistics found in R&B. Roads & Boats takes me about 3.5 hrs and I find it takes about an hour too long, where as this routinely takes me around 3 and I have no issue with the length... Second, it has a more shallow resource conversion chain than R&B (going from logs to stocks takes around 8 buildings plus movements) where as O&L's complicated chains take around 4 or 5 actions/buildings.

Design-wise, it checks a lot of boxes that I like; wide decisions that I wrestle with (especially late game where there are upwards of 30 possible things I could do each turn). Opportunistic resource conversion and action taking, and some long term spatial planning with the settlements. If it had a randomized setup for buildings (e.g. a new expansion that you dealt in half of each game) then it would be a solid. The lack of a fixed end 8 rounds after D in 2p mode is sort of perplexing though and one of the few variants I employ if I have to play it 2p (although, I almost never do).

It also has a bunch of things that I actively dislike about euro games. It's a static setup/play, it doesn't have a lot of interaction (in fact, I've won games basically ignoring most of what other players are doing and instead only ensuring they can't buy the buildings I'm after), and while it is a sandbox of sorts, there are really only a couple patterns for building your settlements in and playing the game sometimes is just a matter of working on the margins of actions and the resource conversions (which are a minority of the point totals). This is true after 5-10 plays and everyone understands how the resources will convert and buildings are scored. It has a fiddly setup (like Bohnanza) where you change what's in the game and how the game is sort of played based on the player count.

I do think it's Uwe's best game, and I still think it's a 9, but it might be my weakest 9.

update: Almost 30 games in, I find I still really enjoy the game, and I think part of it is due to the spatial resource conversion element. One would think I would favor Roads & Boats more for that very reason, but I find R&B to be a fiddly puzzle, not necessarily a *game.* Neuland didn't do what I wanted, and Le Havre doesn't have the spatial piece. This sort of wins by default in that regard.

RATING: 9
A combination of Le Havre, Farmers of the Moor, and new but very Rosenberg-like gameplay.

This game is essentially a worker placement point-grab fest, where you use your workers on your own board area, which you can develop and build up in a somewhat different manner each game.  Actions consist of: 1) grab resources by placing a worker in a building, 2) build a building, 3) grab resources (wood or peat) by clearing your forests or swamps on your play area.  Money can be used to purchase additional landscape tiles as a free action, and it can also be used to hire another player's workers to make them place in their own buildings to generate the resources for you, which can lead to some interesting blocking situations.  There are few ways to score points: 1) building settlements, which are built using food and energy and score points based on their connectivity to other buildings and settlements, 2) built buildings, which provide points, potential settlement points, and special abilities, 3) building wonders, which are like build point buildings with no special function, and 4) accumulating resources that have point values (such as whiskey, gold, etc.).  Unlike most of Rosenberg's games, there is no stress of a "harvest", however you do need to keep up with the neighbours building settlements otherwise you will likely be out the game with respect to points.

The game plays 1-4 players; I've played it 2 and 3 and it scales well, but its a long game and I can't see playing with 4 players.  There do not seem to be any replayability issues whatsoever despite what many BGGers are complaining about; that said, the game does seem like its ripe for expansions and I would certainly welcome expansions.  This is definitely a gamer's game, in line with Rosenberg's other big box games, and its probably his second or third best design for me.  

Own the [boardgame=111193]Loamy Landscape[/boardgame] promo card.

RATING: 7
3 plays / 3,4 players

LIKE: Very long term strategic. I enjoy the resource wheel because it has a bit of push-your-luck to obtain that one extra resource from the wheel, hoping no one takes that wood! Nice spacial puzzle when placing settlements. Good game arc as resource demands change through the phases.

DISLIKE: Chit intensive. Low interaction, but the resource wheel is enough. Brain intensive, so some AP. First time players will not know all the card combos. Some strategies don't change from game to game, therefore if an optimal path is found, the game could get repetitive.

OVERALL: Similar to Le Havre, good game, but can be long. It's a lesser known title than the other Uwe Rosenberg titles. There are two sets of scenarios to keep the game fresh. I still prefer Le Havre, but will play this anytime.

RATING: 9.8
My favorite of the Rosenberg harvest series that I have played.  Certainly feels like a reimplementation of [I]Le Havre[/I] and, in my estimation, an improvement.  Glad to have purchased the 2015 reprint of this that comes with the upgraded [read: not substandard] components.

Very similar to [I]Le Havre[/I] with collecting resources on offer (which accumulate every round that they are not taken) and building buildings to convert/optimize those resources, but with the added twist that the buildings are being built on an [I]Agricola: Farmers of the Moor[/I] landscape (that is expandable to include mountains/coast/water, etc.), so a lot of planning goes into what types of terrain the buildings can be built on, how those buildings are arranged around settlements that are built at defined intervals, whilst maintaining flexibility to build the central religious (cloister) buildings (which have an orthogonal adjacency requirement).  The mechanism for placing workers and taking those workers back adds some very interesting decisions and tension (can't take them back until they are all placed, and one of them, the prior, has a special bonus that he can immediately be used to activate a building when it is built IF he is available), which is a nice change from the pressure of just trying to meet regular feeding requirements.

I don't agree with the criticisms of the game that focus on its lack of variable setup.  The buildings in the game come out in predefined groups, but this is not all that dissimilar from [I]Le Havre[/I] where one typically sees the Clay Mound, or the Cokery, or the Ironworks enter the game at roughly the same stages in the vast majority of games (true, [I]Le Havre[/I] does include a limited number of "Special Buildings" that change from game to game, but I've never seen a winning strategy built around them, partially because there are only one or two optimal paths to take in [I]Le Havre[/I]).  With both the Ireland and France variants in [I]Ora et Labora[/I] plus the seemingly much more varied paths to victory, plus the interesting scoring opportunities based on dwelling values, I think there is enough opportunity for different-feeling games--though it is true that unimaginative gamers may get trapped into doing the exact same thing every single game, if only because it is theoretically possible to do so.  

Theoretically the lack of variable setup means the solo game is "solvable," though I've found this one a harder nut to crack than say, solo [i]Caverna[/i].  Multiple players change up the dynamic enough that I'm not overly worried about multiplayer games falling into the trap of feeling overly similar.

RATING: 8
1st play: Uwe's missing link between Agricola/Le Havre and newer games like Glass Road and Caverna.  The system is a bit rougher here and there are an absurd amount of resource types to convert. The theme and presentation is solid and there is a ton of variety. Huge puzzle to decode, and with no people to struggle to feed you can focus on the wide open victory paths.

2nd play: I really enjoyed the 2nd play of this.  I started making harder resources (whiskey) and using other people's buildings a bit. I could see needing a few plays to really learn the buildings. The wheel makes between round upkeep a breeze, and is a stark contrast to the caver and refill phase. Glad I acquired this one, too bad there probably won't ever be an expansion for it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
1-4(3)

I'm not too much a fan of Loyang, and I haven't bothered (so far) with Merkator, but this really was a great game. It did feel quite a bit like Le Havre, with just a touch of Farmers in the Moor. I think the work contracts are an interesting, if light, bit of interaction (though hiring an opponent's prior can ruin their plans). There is indeed a very wide decision space, including with landscapes and layout and everything else. It's also pretty satisfying to see your monastery at the end of a session.

I do still wish there were some variability. Not that the game needs it ... as I said the decision space is pretty huge ... but because it would potentially create interesting and different dynamics. As in, in certain sessions certain resources and buildings will be in particular demand. This is similar to the building arrangements in Le Havre. The wharf will come out at a different time each session, but you know when it will come out. Early wharf games will play very differently than late wharf games (or other buildings, but notably the wharf can affect the game dynamic most dramatically). I do find this sort of variability really refreshing.

I can understand that perhaps Uwe has tuned the game very specifically to this fixed setup and I think I'll like it just fine as it is. Do I like it more than Le Havre? Potentially. I can understand that people feel it's too similar to Le Havre, and I get some of that feeling myself. They play in maybe about the same time and have very similar game dynamics, but I could see playing either one. Le Havre feels much more rich with resources. Ora & Labora isn't tight, but doesn't blow up like Le Havre.

Do I like it better than Agricola? The setup in O&E is cumbersome, but not Agricola cumbersome. Really, Agricola's still great, though, with great great tension and great variability in the cards and such. All three are 10s, and I'm always up to play, if we have the time.

RATING: 10
A fascinating game with tons of depth and a huge action tree. Takes and streamlines the best mechanisms of Agricola and specially Le Havre and adds a whole new dimension to these games by way of the spatial management of each player's town. In essence, it's an incredibly well crafted economic game: you'll be managing an economy from its humble, farming beginnings as it grows into a monster machine of churning out expensive crafts.

Those who are anguished by the perspective of feeding will also enjoy the fact that the 'feed' phases are cleverly disguised as another opportunity to grow (very profitably), and that you can plan on how much food/energy you will need well in advance.

This is a classic game and, for my tastes, the absolute best Uwe has to offer. A masterpiece.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Another great eurogame from Mr Rosenberg. The game feels a lot like Le Havre because of the many (10+) different resources that you can collect and later transform to another 10+ products. The rules are very simple and the 'meat' of this game is in the buildings. There are many of them each with unique abilities.

So what's new in Ora et Labora??

First of all I like that you now have your own land spaces where you can build things. The whole 'where to put each building in order to maximize the bonus points' problem feels like a small puzzle to me and I like solving puzzles. I also like the visual image of my monastery growing slowly turn after turn.  
The worker placement mechanic is also upgraded with each player having 3 workers one of them with a cool ability to imediately go and activate the building that you just bought. Also you can mess with other players by using their meeples on their buildings.
Oh and the big wheel that keeps track of the resources is simply genious!

The only negative is that the cards are small but to be honest it was neccasary in order to keep the playing area in logical dimensions.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
:arrowE: [u]Synopsis[/u]:
If you like resource conversion, spatial reasoning, and worker placement, I have a hard time considering any game as an exemplar superior to Ora et Labora. Managing a mountain of lovely Klemens Franz-illustrated chits, start with a minuscule plot of land, three monks, and a few resources, then buy buildings, acquire real estate, pay off your opponents, harvest grapes, brew beer, create monastic relics, construct castles, shipping conglomerates, villages, wineries, palaces, and cloisters. 

:arrowE: [u]What I like most[/u]:
The scale and scope. First off, this game will take up a TON of your table, which can be a negative for some. However, I consider the large scale to dovetail well with the large scope of the game, in which from very meager beginnings, you grow your player area not into a simple farmyard with some stables and fields, but a whole freaking diocese. I'm talking mountain ranges, coastlines, foothills, bogs, and forests, all populated with your buildings, settlements, and cloister. When I finish a game of Ora et Labora, I look over all that which I've created, and just gawk at it. It's huge. It tells such a great story. It feels like an accomplishment. In the same way, starting with a few sheep, a piece of wood, and a brick ends up eventually converting to monastic wonders, high-grade ironwork, illustrated libra, fine wine, and a whole host of other top-tier goods. 

:arrowE: [u]What I like least[/u]:
There is a lot to keep track of, primarily the huge swath of chits you acquire which represent your goods. Not only do you need to order them sensibly to note how much you have of each type, but they are double-sided, meaning you can inadvertently mess up what you have with a nudge or flip.

:arrowE: [size=13][url=https://play.spotify.com/user/jwilliamparkin/playlist/4JWklYt2KW8sDMigv8aORx][b]PLAYLIST[/b][/url][/size]

RATING: 7
This is a nice acquire resources and "buildings with cool effects," game with lots of different resources that can be upgraded/improved, and a multitude of engines that lead to victory points available for exploration and multiple plays.  

The worker placement aspect is relatively modest with some interesting, though seemingly small, twists that we only began to explore in our first game.  Am I sensing the possibility of cleverly timed screwage possibilities, or was it just my gaming imagination?

Like virtually every Uwe game, this game is thematically strong, and imbued with a loving devotion to thematic details.  Buildings "do" what their thematic place in the real world would suggest they do.  

Considering the recent profusion of, ahem, certain Feldian style games where the mechanics associated with buildings, resources, or spaces on the game board have essentially no inherent connection to the theme, or the the inherent thematic meaning of these items within the game, it may be time to give Uwe extra praise for his highly thematic designs, and buildings.

RATING: 5
Esta vez Uwe ha pinchado en hueso... al menos conmigo.

La vuelta de tuerca a su temática y mecánica habituales ha sido completamente fallida.

El rondel no aporta nada interesante, si cabe lo hace más farragoso todo. 

El setup realmente un dolor de muelas a la hora de buscar la combinación de partida corta/larga con el país y con el número de jugadores en tres manuales diferentes... saber que reglas aplican a esa combinación y cuales no hace que se te quiten las ganas de jugar antes de empezar.

La interacción entre jugadores es limitadísima, más que en ningún otro título suyo.

Los materiales son mediocres.

Tener todas las cartas de cada etapa disponibles a la vez, el ingente número de recursos diferentes y abrir la posibilidad de utilizar incluso edificios de tus oponentes no ayuda a tener un juego precisamente ágil. Más bien lo contrario... un absoluto despropósito.

Resulta repetitivo turno tras turno, intentando recolectar decenas de puntos y quemándote el cerebro intentando pensar cuál puede ser la mejor estrategia entre los cientos de combinaciones posibles...

Por mucho que trate de reinventar agrícola, no creo que le salga ninguno tan bueno como el primero.

RATING: 9
Played 4P long game, 2P short game x2, and 3P long game.  Try as I may, I can't come up with a Eurogame that comes close on the 2P experience.  Agricola is my all-time favorite board game, and I only kinda like Le Havre.  I feel like this is a mature and FOTM-influenced Le Havre.  Didn't insta-buy a copy because of the well-known production issues, which I hope are resolved with a reprint.  With 25 turns, even a couple of minutes per turn per player can make this run extremely long with 3P and 4P.  I don't even mind it taking forever, but I do mind downtime.  Will check out the 3P and 4P short games.  Update: played a couple 2P games back-to-back and a bit of the shine wore off.  Ireland was a bit too forgiving for both of us, France had some serious point loops (wine for districts, then score districts) which made it so that only one player wanted to end the game while the other caught up.  And I'm growing a bit more concerned about the static starting positions.  Still, picked up the second printing and got a perfect copy.  And I'm still very happy to play it 2P.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Now with the updated 2015 printing, this game has components that match the high quality of gameplay. Like many Uwe Rosenberg's game, players are converting goods multiple times in order to score VP. But to do this, players use their workers (3 per player) to build/use buildings. But there is a catch, players only get their workers back if they place all of them. And one worker (the prior) can be used automatically when building a building. Thus, players attempt to place their workers efficiently by having them all placed before the end-of-the-round and only placing buildings when the prior is available. 

Compared to other Uwe Rosenberg's game, this game doesn't feel as confrontational/mean as Agricola. And because of the rondel mechanism, which gradually increases the number of basic goods/money per round, there always seems something worthwhile to grab. But this game isn't solitaire. Players are able to hire other players' workers and use their builders. And like most worker placement games, if a building is occupied, you can't use it. Thus, players have to contend not only with the race element (grabbing resources) but the prospect of other players' disruptive actions. 

There is more to this game. For instance, there is an abstract scoring element with how buildings are scored at the end of the game. But basically, if you like euro-games and you like resource conversion/management, this is a game you should strongly look at.

RATING: 8
Amit előzetesen tudtam: A 2011-es esseniek közül a top3 játék, és amit sokat hallottam: Agricolába oltott Le Havre.

Ehhez képest szerintem ez tisztán a Le Havre továbbfejlesztése, de olyannyira nagy az átfedés (2/3-os kb.), hogy a pofátlanság határait súrolja szerintem, hogy más néven adták ki (simán lehetett volna Le Havre korongváltozat mondjuk...). Bár amennyi Steam-klónt kiadnak, ahhoz képest ez semmi, de akkor is: kétoldalas nyersanyaglapkák, az épületek mechanikája, de még a grafikai megoldások is (kaja jelölése) gátlástalanul az elődből merít: mentségére szolgál, hogy magától lopott a szerző:)

A játék egyébként szerintem jobb, mint a Le havre, de nem adok rá jobb pontszámot, a sok hasonlóság miatt. Miben jobb: kevésbé van elbonyolítva, kevésbé erőltetett, mint néhány dolog az elődjénél. Mondjam azt, hogy ez populárisabb? A korongon szinte minden infó rajta van, nincs szükség a nagy játéktáblára. Az épületek leépítési mechanizmusa még változatosabbá teszi az egészet. Az a fajta játék, ahol mindig mindenkinek jut valami, még ha bosszankodunk is azon, ha valaki elviszi az orrunk elől (vagy használja) a kiszemelt épületet. Mindig lehet menekülő út, alternatív megoldás, nehéz elképzelni, hogy nyerő stratégia lenne a játékban. A perjel nagyon tetszett, nemcsak a neve miatt. Hasonlóan az előd(ök)höz, itt is max. sejteni lehet, hogy ki áll jól, a végén érhetik meglepetések az embert.
Amit még kiemelnék, a két külön játékmód: ír/francia. Teljesen más épületek, némileg eltérő nyersanyagok.

A szabály bár nem rövid, nem is bonyolult szerintem. A játék szinte nyelvfüggetlen, ne riasszon senkit az angol, de még a német se! Játékidő szűk 3 óra volt nekünk 4 fővel. A minőséggel kapcsolatban voltak kritikák, de ha jól láttam ez orvosolva lett...

RATING: 8
One of Uwe's playtesters made a comment about having played a game he felt was similar to Le Havre, but on steroids. That very comment was erroneously attached to Merkator before its release, and contributed heavily towards the crowds disappointment about that game.
Now, the game that was the rightful destination of that comment has come out. It is Ora et Labora.
And the thing that playtester said is SO TRUE: Uwe is finally back on track, with another masterpiece!

Ora et Labora has many things in common with Le Havre: very diversified paths to victory points, increasingly-powerful buildings becoming available during the game, the buildings being usable by every player, but at a cost when they belong to someone else.

There's lots of differences, also: no need to feed one's own people, no loans, a meaningful puzzle aspect in laying out the buildings (not to mention "the wheel": it can save many precious minutes of playtime!). I can't see a reason for not having both Le Havre and Ora et Labora inside the collection!

This is a game that will not storm BGG like Agricola and Le Havre did 4 years ago, but it is undeniably a game of the same caliber (I suspect it is the better one of the three) because the board game scene is more varied and frenzied now, and Ora et Labora is less innovative than games such as Eclipse.

But, after just three plays, I feel the need to play it again to explore its intricacies, and begin to master it. Let's go pray and work!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
First Impression: This game relies heavily on the resource conversion mechanism. There's also a spatial awareness requirement to the game, as placing buildings beside other buildings create synnergies which score VPs. Also, the ability to use other people's buildings adds a level of interaction.

RATING: 8.7

RATING: 4
Cold as steel in a winter’s night, Ora et labora is a «multiplayer solitaire» like many other eurogames are: interaction between players is kept to a minimum and, to prevent them from getting too close, not even trades are allowed. Actually the game’s focus is just on collecting different kinds of resources, too many of them: luckily a nice parking disc comes in handy and helps keep track of the different amounts available throughout the game.

As one would expect of any eurogame, everything here works fine: it is more of a cold mathematical operation than an involving experience though, so one really has to love this kind of games to wish to play Ora et labora; everyone else will quickly find himself going adrift and struggling in a chilly ocean of resource collection and management.

In all honesty I can’t rate Ora et labora any higher.

On a side note, I couldn’t help but notice (and like) the in-game joke on a wood card showing a blue-dressed dancing man resembling Tom Bombadil as depicted by Tim Hildebrand in a renowned drawing of the 70’s. Really a nice touch.

If you know Agricola, Inland Port, or other games and then check this one, you say oh no, again?...

RATING: 8.7
Really looking forward to this one - it has the beauty of Agricola with (hopefully!) less of the frustrating blocking that I loathe so much about that latter game.

Can't wait for English publication.

EDIT: It has arrived, and after 1 play was everything I expected. Unlike Agricola, it is very open-ended, and you are not punished for stepping outside the 'normal' route to victory. There are also none of the stupid cards that make every game unpredictable and unbalanced.

Interaction is quite high, and very subtle. In fact, of all the games I've played that are criticised as 'multiplayer solitaire', I'd say this one deserves the label least. You constantly have to watch your opponents' tableaux, and remain aware of what buildings and resources they have, and which ones they are going for.

I really enjoyed my play of this one last night, it might be the Uwe game I actually enjoy...

EDIT2: after four plays, 2x2p, 1x3p and 1x4p, I can say this:

There is a lot more depth to this game than Agricola, and that's saying something. The decision tree is wonderfully open and flexible. there are subtle differences between different parts of different resource trees (IE, the difference between getting one whiskey/beer with the lighthouse, and multiple if you've got enough grain with the brewery/malthouse). Al of this makes Agricola's basic game look bland by comparison, and even the with the cards looks less complex than basic Ireland Ora.

I'm really enjoying discovering the multiple paths to victory - it all seems phenomenally well-balanced, specialising in one thing (say, wonders vs. settlements) produces VERY tight games, and it feels like the trick is efficiencies, not that one strategy is weaker. Part of playing this game is about making sure you have maximised ingredients so resource conversion is quick. This might mean the game is "solvable" but with the variety of available buldings in different versions of the game I doubt it'll be a worry. I feel like I've barely scratched the surface.

I really hope this one, like Agricola, has a bunch of expansions.

RATING: 8
Le Havre meets Agricola, sort of. Ewe does it again. A lot of familiar elements: collecting resources, managing real-estate, building stuff, action-selection, all to basically create a VP engine. While familiar in some respects, Ewe has rearranged the elements to work in some unique ways making the game very much its own thing.

This game comes with oodles of bits and there is a lot of set-up required - maybe almost a little too much - the layout makes keeping track of what is available out there to "use" (other player's buildings) more difficult than in Le Havre. 

I enjoyed it well enough but it didn't strike me as a "must have," given I have the other two already and they haven't yet lost their charm. I'm surprised at how quickly and how high it jumped up on the Geek List. I don't think it is as good as either of its "uncles."

RATING: 9
Del creador de Agricola o Le HAvre, es un juego en que cada jugador tiene que gestionar su diócesis construyendo edificios, utilizandolos para transformar unos recursos en otros y ampliando su dominio. El juego tiene muchos recursos diferentes, pero su gestión durante el juego se lleva a cabo de una forma muy cómoda gracias a un inteligente rondel que va haciendo que la cantidad disponible de un recurso se vaya incrementado a medida que avanza la partida (si nadie lo toma claro).

En cuanto a mecánicas, me ha recordado mucho a Le Havre, por aquello de transformar unos recursos en otros. También toma del agricola la importancia de la ubicación de los edificios (las colonias puntuan segun lo que tengan alrededor). Me ha parecido muy entretenido y en todo momento tienes multiples formas de conseguir lo que quieres.. y tienes que escoger la que te reporta mayores ganancias. Además, también puedes utilizar los trabajadores de la diócesis de otros jugadores (les contratas).

En fin. Un juegazo como la copa de un pino. No es apto para no jugones. Con Gema ni me lo planteo... pero cualquier jugón creo que quedará encantado con el juego.

Me lo regaló Marc por mi cumpleaños, en Julio 2013. Ya se había ido a Bélgica, de modo que me cogió por completo  de sorpresa. Junto al Troyes, son los dos juegos más valorados de mi colección. Además de por el juego en sí, por que me los regaló un gran tipo.

Con gran dolor de mi corazón, lo vendí el 7/5/2015 a anguss, a través de la bsk. No tenía muchas posibilidades de jugarlo y andaba corto de cash, y tenía que hacer caja para pagar las CLBSK2015 (lo que me costó un disgusto con Gema) y tuve que poner unos cuantos títulos en venta. Lo vendí por 42€ con kiala incluido. Un regalo. 
Cuadno esté en mejores situaciones, me gustaría volver a conseguirle... más que por el juego en sí,que me gusta mucho, por los recuerdos que me trae.

- the shoddy components
- the building cards which really need to be memorized in order to play.   Unfortunately we play it so irregularly that we need to relearn all of those card combinations each time we play.

RATING: 8
I was hoping that O&L would be the Rosenberg-lovers Rosenberg game, or at least, my favorite of his designs.  All the elements are there: conversion mania, open information, many paths to victory, none of which seem overbearing.  Yes, I certainly love games that revolve around converting things efficiently, and this offers plenty of conversions, and with nothing hidden, it should scratch that efficiency itch... very efficiently.

Sadly, though, the game falls short.  It is still a great game by one standard: I am immersed throughout.  Playing it is fun.  The problem, though, is that the conversion trees for this thing are too complex, with too many variables to mentally juggle.  Yes, the different paths to victory (buildings, settlements, and chits) seem balanced, but are they?  How can anyone tell - without investing days doing computations?  This is my main problem.  All the openness of the game leads to opacity, not clarity.  You have no standard by which to judge how good your performance was.  Certainly, at the end of the game, you recognize you could have done better, or been more efficient... but where?  

The problem is that you are constantly juggling short, mid, and long-term strategies, without knowing which one is the most efficient use of your time during that particular turn.  You can feel it out, intuit it, to some degree, but at the end of the game, you have that Power Grid feeling.  Oh, I won by 10 points?  Well, I must have done something right - but understanding what is pretty much impossible.  Without learning, every game becomes a set of semi-random initial choices (well, I'll start out concentrating on money this game) that continually butterfly out.  You try to surf the tactical wave as best you can, but that's about as good as it gets.  Yes, there are many turns where you could do a cost/benefit analysis of the best way to proceed for that particular turn, or even two, three, or four turns into the future... but a two player game is probably 35-40 turns long!  So the whole thing feels consistently opaque, consistently "new" every time.  There is always more you could analyze, but at a certain point, why bother?  This is not my full-time job.

Compared with Agricola and Le Havre, it lacks tension.  I don't mind, because as I said, I'm continually immersed, and I also find that some of the tension in those other games is false.  (In Le Havre, you can get along without feeding, and in Agricola, once you know the game enough, there are many categories you can consistently max out).  At any rate, it has more the feel of Loyang, but at least in Loyang the opacity is due to the hidden aspects of the game - the card draft, for instance.  So overall, Loyang gets the edge.  I'd like to be able to understand how to play better, but other than some intuitions that accumulate, I don't see how.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
A very good game, even better than Agricola, IMO. Everyone talks about the production wheel as being the novel element here, but the real difference from Le Havre/Agricola is the spatial mechanic of scoring points based on where you place your buildings. This is why I like the game, because you really feel like you are building a colony, clearing forests and draining swamps, expanding the cloisters and attaching a network of towns and villages on a map. The insane number of different goods also leads to more complex chains of production in a way that surpasses Rosenberg's other games. I'd love to see an expansion.

RATING: N/A
Secret Santa
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Play 3 was the two-player game, and my feelings were much more positive than the first two plays with 4. I think the smaller game space is more comprehensible. Like Le Havre, you need to be focused on efficiency and high-value items, i.e., Wonders. So figure out how you're going to get them.

Like all the late-model Rosenberg games, it's won on pacing, knowing the pulse of the game, when to "cash in" a big efficiency play, and how much of any given resource you're really going to need. The pulse of Ora et Labora is, I think, harder to find than Le Havre or Agricola which are pretty straightforward. Not sure what that means.

The endgame for the 2-player game isn't great. You can actually be in a position, I think, where the game can get badly drawn out - there are multiple buildings still available to be built, but they're low-value and both players are doing better by cranking their engines, and the player who stops cranking to do the last low-value build will lose. Not sure. Too bad it doesn't have a fixed endpoint.
---
Couple plays in, I think it's too much - too many options, too flat a development tree. I wanted to like it, but I think it's just too overwhelming.
---
Hmm. On the one hand, I definitely don't get the "Le Havre+" vibe that some speak of. Yes, it's Rosenberg doing his thing, the thing he's been doing since Agricola, and if you expect him to be striking out in new directions, he's not. But he's a designer who likes his tropes, and there was no reason to expect he would. To me, Ora et Labora feels, mechanically, like a good theme-and-variations on what he's been doing successfully for a while now. You know what you're going to get, but it's changed up and the tensions are different than they are in his previous games.

What I'm less enthralled about is that the narrative tension seems be much less deftly handled here than in his previous games. Both Agricola and Le Havre nicely build, with constrained options at first that open up over time and aren't overwhelming. Ora et Labora hits you with great huge whacks of complexity at irregular intervals, then leaves you alone for a while, and it's very hard to digest or get any sense of. Also, unlike all his other games, Ora et Labora has no variability - it's all on the table from turn 1, and doesn't change from game to game. OK, but it makes it feel crushing for new players and for your first time out.

I think there is good stuff here, but I wish the game had the same attention to narrative that made Agricola so compelling. I also with it were a bit shorter but had more focussed play.

I'd suggest your first time out, you probably want to play with 2 or 3. With 4, it's a very complicated game, and can easily run long.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I think the description of a merge between Agricola and Le Havre is pretty apt here.  I'm a pretty big fan of Agricola, and not so hot on Le Havre, and I liked this game some where in the middle.

The goal in Ora is to build buildings, which you can then utilize to make goods and convert goods into other goods, eventually using them to build more buildings/score points.  You can also use other players' buildings for a fee, so you're not dead in the water if someone builds a building that was central to your strategy!

Overall, a good game in the "make stuff and convert it into other stuff to convert into points" genre.  If you think that sort of game is too dry, this game isn't for you. but if you like this sort of optimization game, it's a good one!

Need to get this to the table more often; multiple paths to victory

RATING: 8
Another of Uwe Rosenberg's big resource-conversion Euros! I really enjoyed the spatial puzzle of placing your buildings and settlements in the proper environments and maximizing adjacency points. The resource wheel was also a really neat idea and I liked that it wasn't as punishing as some of his other games. The game did suffer from resource overload though. There were just too many resources and trying to find the right chits was just slowing the game down too much on our first play. I also wish there were a bit more variability form game to game as far as buildings go. Regardless, a very good Rosenberg that fans of brainy Euros will definitely enjoy!

RATING: 9
Rated 7 after one play. So, admittedly, I was not terribly excited to try this one. I generally like games that have harsh systems or that force choices instead of offering a wide open playground with dizzying ranges of choices. Le Havre falls into that latter category, and so when Ora et Labora was described to me as similar, right down to the ability to use other people's buildings, I figured it wasn't for me.

This was better than I expected, and better than Le Havre. For whatever reason, this seems to have a better theme, but it preserves that resource conversion/improvement that I like about Le Havre. The placement of buildings is interesting. One play is not enough to assess, but count me as interested in a 2nd play.

Update: I kept thinking about that first game, and actually picked this up via a trade. Now the goal will be to get some more plays of this title.

Update: So, tried this as a solo game (487 points, Ireland). Not bad... great way to learn the buildings and the waves of resource conversion and scoring options, which is probably the biggest barrier to entry. I might try this with the France side, but that's probably the only solo playing I'll do with this game.

Update: 496 solo points with France. Close, but no cigar (or baguette).

Update: 501 solo with Ireland, and that probably wraps up any solo play.

Update: two more plays now, and I'm bumping this up to an 8. I keep wanting to play this game, even though I'm not terribly good at it! A couple observations... the sheer diversity of resources keeps this a challenge, but when you couple that with the placement of settlements, this will really make your brain melt. The game really tells a nice story from start to finish, so the game's length is totally justified. I don't know about other groups, but we're banging out a game in about two hours.

Update: ... and I've now played this game a dozen or so times. Every play reveals something new to me, and I grow fonder of this game with each play. This is truly a keeper.

RATING: 6
My word, where to begin...? Pretty much pure crunch, in every manner, and very complex - most certainly not for neophytes of the hobby or those after a light-hearted session. Not my favourite of Rosenburg's harvest games... but I can appreciate the solid underlying mechanics. I have seeen many call it a sandbox game... and while it does put me in mind of a medieval Sim City - hundreds of ways to score, it is still limited in only offering a few desired goals. Plus, in-keeping with this idea, it is nice to see a range of artwork on the forest/peat bog terrain cards - something that would be easy to skimp on...

Could have done with several more buildings for the beach, water, hill, and mountain territories, as there are so very few included - but it looks like no add-on is forthcoming.

One thing I think could be changed is randomising the buildings that can be built on each round, rather than having them all available all the time - add a little luck and strategy into a game that otherwise has very little. And without them, the sandbox aspect does become somewhat of a tedious grind... which is a true shame.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
When I played Le Havre, I was profoundly bored by it.  The mechanics were fine (actually, it was so long ago that I don't remember how it plays anymore), but I felt incredibly bogged down by just having a ridiculous number of items to pay attention to.

Ora & Labora is similar, but with many more interesting mechanics to keep me from feeling worn out before the final rounds.  Primarily, I felt the method of obtaining basic materials a la nifty wheel was an interesting way of re-packaging things like Puerto Rico's "add a buck" method of incentivising unused actions, and the yellow cards and village bonus cards (as well as additional game boards) made building placement a much more important and fun aspect of the game.

The pawn usage also added a clever method of control to the board as well - do we tie up opponents' boards to keep them from using their actions, or do we fill our own to keep our priory available for those powerful free actions?  Is it worth paying out a coin (or two) for those other actions, or will our own serve well enough?

But, overall, the thing that dragged it down was the same thing as Le Havre, albeit to a lesser extent: I don't want to have to look at everybody's board (and often, every single card).

My real problem with games like this is that it's too easy to lead me, not even really to AP, but just to time wasting because I'll likely want to see every card - I can skim the cards and risk missing one or misunderstanding the benefits, or I can spend the time required to read every single one available to me.  I have to balance my desire to not feel like I'm wasting a lot of time with my desire to not feel like I may have just made an obvious oversight.  Both of these tend to lead to unfortunate experiences, but at least I don't have a lot of these choices to make until a lot later in the game (which, if I recall correctly, was not the case in Le Havre).  I'll play this again if it comes up, but will not suggest it.

RATING: 6
Two games in:
Didn't live up to my unrealistic expectations. Not a bad game, but still prefer Le Havre. The O&L wheel is a nifty idea, but in practice doesn't really improve the game to me. In fact it seems easier to add a couple resources every turn than to count out a large number of resources when you take them. And it's easier to make mistakes when taking large numbers. I liked the extreme simplicity of taking resources being your turn. In O&L now you have to use a worker even when doing that. It's not a big deal, but still. But more importantly, I miss the pacing of Le Havre, the burden of feeding your people every round, the multiple ways of doing it, the nuances of loans, of building sequencing, of special buildings. I don't like that there is no random setup in O&L. The spacial aspects in O&L don't seem to make up the difference for what I miss from LH. Sure, where you put stuff matters, but it's a bit puzzly, where you just arrange things for the most vp. Where you put them doesn't affect gameplay at all.

RATING: 6
Two plays: 2 player game and 4 player game.  

Good: The rondel is unbelievably awesome.  Simply a great upkeep management tool. Also, the spatial component of the game gives it more depth and flavor than Le Havre.  

Bad: Too long.  AP is not as long in 2-player, but in 4-player (where you only have 1 action a turn) the AP becomes pretty brutal because the choices are simply overwhelming at times.  Player interaction is very sparse. It's also hard to see other people's buildings.  And as for the buildings, memorizing them and when they come out will help you do well.    

Conclusion: Better than Le Havre in every way. It's less cutthroat than Agricola.  So Ora > Agricola > Le Havre.

RATING: 8
Ora et Labora clearly is an update of Le Havre. Le Havre's set-up is partially random. Although the randomness is minimal, it is sufficient to vary the play from game to game. Ora et Labora includes no randomness, so variable play is produced by inundating the players with buildings and goods, by including two variants (Ireland and France), and by introducing a placement puzzle.

I guess the idea is that figuring out how to coordinate the goods, buildings, and placements will create a decision tree so large that play never will become scripted. Could be, but that approach also makes the game directionless. In Le Havre, the goal is clear, namely, build up a cache of steel going into the final turn. In OetL, it's not at all clear what you should be doing.

Fun, but a notch below Le Havre, in my view, and longer, too, believe it or not.

RATING: 7
[2012-11-28 Rating: n.a. -> 7]
Since I do enjoy Agricola and Le Havre, I was looking really forward to Ora et Labora. Now that I've played it twice, my enthusiasm has faded a bit. I still think that Ora et Labora is a fine game that I'll hopefully play again, but all in all this game has just too much of everything for my taste. 

There are a ton of different buildings you can build so after a while, the options become overwhelming. It's like you can combine the actions of all of your deck's cards in Warhammer Invasion at once. On top of it, you have the spatial element that you have to manage. This is coupled with a long playtime and and a lot of downtime. I guess, all this things can be overcome with more experience, but I fear that Ora et Labora is just too long and feels too much like work in the early stages for my gaming group. So sadly, I don't think that we'll become experienced enough to reach a new level of enjoyment. But nevertheless, if you do like heavy euro games, give this one a try. I guess I'll stick to Agricola and Le Havre as they feel more streamlined.

On a sidenote: Somehow I like the resource piling in Le Havre better than the resource wheel. You just grab the pile and your done. No counting. But that's just my personal preference.

RATING: 4.5
I get torn on games like this.  I had a fun time playing it, because I enjoy the people that were playing with me.  But this is not my favorite type of game.  It has so many options, that the winner is often the person willing to take up more of the other player's time by looking at more of the available options. RATING: 6
Players are monks in medieval France or Ireland (two variants to play).  Players take actions to use a building, clear land, or build a building.  Clearing land gives you wood or peat; using building gets you other resources, or turns resources into more advanced stuff; building buildings gives you victory points and cheap access to that building's function.  Every few turns, game play is interrupted to allow player's to build a settlement - a building that does nothing but earn victory points for lucrative adjacent buildings at the end of the game.  Big, long game of producing stuff, and churning it through the buildings into other stuff.  If you are a Rosenberg big game fan, you'll lap it up.  I'm a Rosenberg "take it or leave it" guy, and I'm not so convinced.  It feels like the other big games he's done, with a few tweaks and new artwork.  On the plus side, there are solo and fast play variants.  Good for a few plays, then off to the marketplace, I suspect.

RATING: 9
A flawed gem.

There are some serious UI issues.   It is almost impossible to see what buildings my neighbors have even when sitting right next to them.   The cards are just too small.  Even 25% bigger would have helped.   Or maybe the icons on the cards could have been done better.   The magazine paper reference sheets, the postcard-thin player boards and extensions...  it all feels very cheap for a $70 retail game.   The wood bits and central dial are very nice, on the other hand.  Too bad the whole package wasn't executed to that level.

Speaking of the dial...   it removes SOME fiddlyness from the game, but certainly not all of it.   There are dozens of chits, and you will be adding them to the bank or withdrawing them in reams at a time throughout the game.   So, still plenty of fiddle here.  Don't be fooled by the clever dial.

But the game itself...   wow!  It is one of those games that keeps you thinking about it nonstop, even days after playing.   The amount of options towards the end becomes staggering, so you won't want to play with AP-prone people or it could become excrutiating.   It seems like a game that is best left to gut instinct and some careful planning without excessive over-calculation.

Gets better with every play, as the UI issues tend to fade away and everyone becomes familiar with the cards.  Zero luck.   No downtime, as you are always thinking and planning ahead when it isn't your turn.   Shines in both 2 and 3-player modes.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.25
My 3rd favorite of the whole Uwe series.  Ora has a few twists that make it stand out over the similar titles.  It has the same qualities as Le Havre of being able to use other players' buildings, but there aren't quite as many to worry about.  It also feels to me like the resource types and conversions are easier to manage, but that may just be my imagination.  The real difference is that instead of being forced to pay set amounts of food, you're encouraged to build scoring structures (which just happen to cost lots of food).  You can get away with lower-value 'harvests', but this will have a negative impact on your score.  
At the same time, you're trying to build up a network of buildings into which these scoring structures will fit, like atoms in a crystal lattice.  This positional puzzle is good fun, and there are some other wrinkles, like trying to match terrain types, buy & clear land, keep your monastery contiguous, and so on.  About my only complaint is that there are some of the terrain types that aren't really useful, since there's only a single building that can go there (ex. the houseboat on the water or the castle in the mountains).

RATING: 8
Ora et Labora is basically a re-imaginging of Le Havre but it does have elements of Agricola in there as well.  How do they compare?

Things I prefer in OeL when compared with Agricola:

-Equal distribution of workers with no chance of having more having more than anyone else.
-No forced strategy diversification that can result in negative points for failure
-No forced feeding phase that can result in negative points for failure

Things I liked about Agricola that OeL incorporates:

-Having forests and peat take up spaces on individual player boards

Things I prefer in OeL when compared with Le Havre:

-I don't find any given resource becomes useless in OeL, even in the late game.
-I like that it's harder to block players off from specific building use.  If someone squats a worker on their own building in Le Havre (ie Wharf) there's nothing you can do about it until they move.
-No mandatory feeding phase [b]every damn round[/b].  That being said, failing to feed and taking a loan wasn't as bad as the begging card in Agricola.

Things I liked about Le Havre that OeL incorporates:

-Agricola does have resource conversion but it's to a much lesser extent than Le Havre.  Thankfully OeL is closer to Le Havre in this respect.
-It could be argued that LH's energy and OeL's fuel are the same kind of resource.  Agricola's fuel is a bit different than energy, but OeL acquires fuel the same way: through peat on your board.  Well, some buildings as well.
-I like that LH let's you focus on whatever strategy you want without penalty unlike Agricola and OeL continues in the same vein
-I like that OeL stayed similar to Le Havre in that you are creating buildings and using them to mostly do resource conversion and that you're able to use opponents buildings at a cost.

"Ok, so you complain about the Feeding phases of Agricola and Le Havre, but not the Settlement phase Ora et Labora? You do realize you probably won't win if you don't settle!"

While that is true you should probably settle every phase you have an opportunity to do so to have a decent shot at winning, it's still less constraining in OeL compared to Gric and LH.  Why?  OeL gives you multiple settlements with different costs.  So when a settlement phase comes up it's not like in Agricola "oh damn I have to get food or I lose" or Le Havre "I have to have this much money/food or start taking loans."  OeL is more like, "ok, so a settlement phase is coming up, I need a minimum of X to play this small settlement but if I can attain these resources I could get this higher cost one out."  It's a completely different feeling.  OeL's seems the lease stressful, LH is closer to OeL and Agricola's is just stressful.

So, what sets Ora et Labora apart?

-The spatial element of your buildings dwelling values in relationship to your settlements, your cloister buildings having to be adjacent to one another, and what kind of land requirements are needed to be able to place certain kinds of buildings/settlements.  When thinking spatially, you'll need to purchase additional land as well as clear spots of forest and peat to be able to open up places for your buildings and settlements.  The order and choices in which you do all of these spatial issues in conjunction with the the ongoing resource game makes this a deliciously heavy game.

-The resource wheel.  I'm not going into detail about this but the way it makes everything in the game flow compared to Gric and LH, [i]this is innovation[/i].

-Resources as stated earlier don't generally tend to become useless.  Up until LH and gric, you had: food, building resources, money, and energy.  Not only does OeL pull this off stunningly, you can now convert resources into various resources that are worth varying amounts of VP.  If that wasn't enough, depending on what types of VP resources you have, you can possibly convert them into a Mega VP tile called a Wonder.

-If all that wasn't enough, you can play the game two different ways: Ireland and France.  Each one has different buildings and resources available within it along some commonalities between each of the two versions.

This is excellent.

RATING: 6
Call me an old fogey but I remember when eurogames were simple and elegant like Modern Art and Ra. Agricola and Le Havre may be more complicated compared to those classics but Ora et Labora takes the cake on complexity. 

It's not a terrible game - the base Uwe engine is there and it still works but in Ora, it's easy to be overwhelmed by all the possibilities. And it all kicks off right from the beginning - no easing in or what have you. Players must juggle not just their economic/VP engine but also manage "tile-placement" of their buildings. It's all a little bit much.

Also, with four players, the game is really long - as long as a mid-complexity wargame, which for a euro is too ridiculous.

RATING: 7
I had very high hopes going into this game since I am a big fan of Uwe Rosenberg.  I was elated when I saw the components and really liked the press your luck feel of the wheel mechanic. There are a ton of resources which lends the game to making multiple paths to victory if you try to focus on a few specialty areas- and that's great and interesting and fun.  But, what didn't do it for me was the puzzley nature of arranging your cloister to maximize points. now I thought this was going to be a peripheral kind of thing, but I quickly found out in my first at and everyone thereafter that building placement is a very significant part of the game. So arranging your board and building in certain areas and saving money to buy your multiplier cards is hugely important and will dictate a lot of your actions.  The game rewards repeated plays because knowing those core cards and how they will multiply your victory points is HUGE.  But it felt like an annoying puzzle that you had to regularly adapt.  I would've liked more variety In the core buildings, as well.  Perhaps something more asymmetrical? Anyway, it's a solid game, but not one Im a huge fan of... I'll stick with caverna and Le Havre!

RATING: 7
This is a huge resource conversion puzzle chopped up into tiny bits, and your job is to reassemble a better picture than your opponents. The conceit here is the forced contract innovation to the worker placement whereby you can use an opponent’s piece to visit a building for you (for a fee, of course). The challenge is in identifying an efficient conversion path via player buildings. They may be your buildings or they may belong to other players, but you need to identify that path and make it work just a little better than your opponents. In a shift from Agricola, there’s an optional Settlement phase instead of an obligatory feeding phase.

RATING: 6
[2016 March 02; 5 plays; 6/10]
My first play of this blew my mind. It was a full-length solo play and lasted something like 3 hours, all the while, I loved the ability to take my time and fully analyze the perfectly track-able information. Mind you that I had only played a small amount of games at this time, but it immediately shot into my top 5. Now, 4 plays later of the 2 and 3 player short games, I'm bored.

Uniquely, it has a huge pool of resources (some >20 resources), uses a rondel for simple upkeep, has no randomness, and some of the coolest sandbox elements in a board game I've seen (expanding land ownership and city-building for additional work placement spots). Points are made by constructing buildings in your monastic landscape, and then using those buildings to gain and convert resources. The buildings players use typically produce a unique set of resources, or they do some unique resource conversion, and players can pay one another to use their landscape's buildings.

All of this is rather boring for a 3 hour game though! The competition is indirect, non-destructive, and largely non-aggressive. The primary exercise is to perform min-max calculations of resource conversion, where complications occur when another player blocks you, but there are so many paths to victory that it's (a) difficult to predict the path your opponent is attempting to take (in comparison to games with less choices) and (b) when one does block an opponent, there are still plenty of other, rather viable choices. I find this ultimately boring!

RATING: 6
[b]Wonderful, complex goods-exchange game with low-stress. No variable setup, which could hurt game after 20+ plays. The basics of the game are more fun than Le Havre, but Le Havre's variability is attractive.

Rating history: 10|9|9|10|9|9|10|10|9|8

I traded this away only because a friend still owns it, and I still own Le Havre. I got good value in the math trade.[/b]


JUNE 2014 - After two dozen games of Le Havre (which possesses a very similar central mechanic, and is designed by the same person) and after doing tons of research before trading for Ora & Labora, I am stoked to be playing this Ora.

For my tastes, Ora is the better game of the two - with setting and artwork I prefer, and a wide open strategic tree. Le Havre has a small advantage with the variable set-up, and yet the game still plays out quite samey to me, with several main branches to race for VPs (build and ship). I LOVE that Ora takes away feeding, ships, and shipping, and replaces it with acquiring various land plots, spatial placement, and more goods to pursue victory with. 

My gut feeling: I will get at least 20 good plays out of this, keep it in my collection for a couple years, and never rate it lower than a 9.

Since it looks like there will never be an expansion for this, I'm in the process of designing one (an Upper Canada variant with westward expansion, native contact, etc.). Feel free to drop me a line if you're interested in helping me test/develop it.

July 2014: Finally got a 3er going, and it was a blast. Including teaching two new players, we were done in less than 2h30m. The end game is MUCH different in 3p games: you must be much more focused near game-end, and have fewer overall game actions. Makes the end-game of the 2p short version seem clunky and almost broken (a player with a good engine near game end won't end the 2p game in order to generate more points).

9th play: 9. OCT/14. I think that's my last play of 2p. It's a good game, but just doesn't shine with 2, in my opinion. Best with 3p. I wish I could mash the variability of Le Havre with the spatial placement and no-feeding-necessary of Ora & Labora. I would have the 2nd best game of all time on my hands.

10th play: 9. Oct/14. First time on France board, and really starting to understand the importance of focus for point generation. Building is always good, but generating points... Gotta get'er done. Taught Dewald, who also enjoyed it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
Zman arriv 1-2012

Players: 1-4
Duration: 3 hrs
Type: economic & city building

I wish Ora & Labora were a completely different game, which it isn't--it has strong elements of Le Havre, and to a lesser degree, Agricola.  Le Havre has similar bits, conversions of resources, and building.  In Agricola resources build up and then someone gets to them before you do.  Despite the similarities, this plays very differently, and I like the play. 

Pros:  Replay-ability with two different countries to play and short and advanced games.  I like the scoring of the settlements, and the various avenues to gaining points.  

Cons:  It is fiddly, even more than Le Havre.   The boards of of poor quality--thin cardboard, not the heavy boards in Agricola.  Like Le Havre, lots of two-sided chips--one side with an item, the other side with the item upgraded.  But unlike Le Havre, the two sides are of completely different colors and we all were continually getting misled and mixing the chips up. 

Uwe Rosenberg is making some very strong games.  My order of ranking for the series:
Gates of Loyang
Le Havre
Ora & Labora
Agricola

(I am also a great fan of his Merkator.)

RATING: 7
Massive game that feels a lot like Le Havre, with even more resource types and so many different ways to score.

It's a pseudo worker placement that involves timing more than priority; the ability to place opponents' workers is interesting and no doubt much more important than we understood.

There's a constant feeling that everything coming out is so much better. The positive settlement phase replaces the negative pressure of feeding but is still just as critical to score.

The inclusion of Ireland/France is mindblowing.

RATING: 9.5
I was very excited to try Ora et Labora as Le Havre is in my list of top 10 games, and I heard that this might even be better.  Now that I’ve played this “upgrade”, I’m not sure if I’d call it better, but it’s certainly right up there.   I like having 3 workers to place instead of just the one, and the goods selection wheel is genius.  I also appreciate the addition of the spatial element that the village cards bring, and the idea of having to pay extra to get enough room to play certain types of cards. There are a lot of cards to get familiar with, and many, many ways to score points – so much so that it’s almost overwhelming to try and figure out how all of the various resources work together.  But once a player is able to map out many of the potential routes to victory, it does work together very well.

RATING: 6
In a way, Ora et Labora is merely a friendlier version of Le Havre. Players collect resources which they then use to construct buildings which then provide additional action choices and/or points. The similarities don't top at superficial thematic elements, though. There are resource "stacks" that grow until somebody grabs them, at fixed intervals you need additional food and/or fuel to extend your village, and you can pay your fellow players to use one of their buildings.

Simply said, if you already have Le Havre you probably don't need Ora et Labora. So what makes Ora et Labora "more friendly?" For one, it is shorter than its ancestor, though probably not by much. It also feels a little more forgiving because the feeding phase is kind of optional. If you have (and pay) the resources you gain an additional extension to your village, if you don't nothing happens. This effect is mostly psychological, and it's there simply because there is no punishment other than not scoring additional points.

Finally, even though the tiny building cards make it more difficult than it needs to be for new players, Ora et Labora has less variability. There is always the same set of buildings entering play at the exact same time (well, the game actually includes two different sets of buildings, but that's beside the point) so after a game or two they should be familiar and there is nothing to dramatically alter the game balance like some of the special buildings in Le Havre do.

RATING: 4
This game is basically Le Havre with a positioning puzzle and a crapload of extra buildings, almost all of which get constructed, and in which it is really necessary to keep track of who has what building positioned where. Considering that the cards are so small, and that large numbers of chits (with two different sides) are strewn over the table, it is hard to see what your opponents are doing, or actually who is doing well. To me, this game is a paradigm for the "modern" game in which the designer supplies the players with hundreds of options and dozens of ways to score points, without much rhyme or reason, and then says: "you make your own fun with this mess." The mediocre design combined with the deplorable physical attributes make this one of the worst games I ever played. Definitely at the bottom of the Rosenberg-style games.

RATING: 8
I've always been a fan of what Rosenberg has done with board games, especially the Harvest series of games. OeL is another one of those "gain resources; trade them for better resources; trade those better resources for points." While Le Havre and Loyang have felt distinctively different from Agricola (while still containing that Harvest nugget) both Caverna and OeL are amalgams (with OeL owing more to Le Havre than Agric). It's a solid game, with a good rondel mechanic to keep things tracked. Nice, expandy board, familiar conversion system, and little differences here and there. This isn't distinct enough from Le Havre that I think it's broken new ground; both Caverna and this one feel like Rosenberg has tried to make amends with aspects of his former games that he wished to improve on after publication. That being said, none of his originals feel outdated, nor do his fresh ones feel revolutionary. Rosenberg keeps doing his thing, and I'm fine with that, though I do wish for a new direction at some point.

RATING: 8
This is a fairly complex game but a lot of fun to play. There's tons of paperwork in the game, but once you figure out what they're for it's very well organized. The game play itself is a puzzle when playing solo (which I have played) and if you can score 500 points you win. My first game was only 255 which was a horrible score, but getting there was just a whole lot of fun. You have so many choices for placing your pawns and taking your actions that you could go into analysis paralysis which is easy for me to do. It will have some replay value as solo for quite a few games since the building cards are two sided (giving you an Irish and French variant), but since the cards always come out the same initially I suspect it would have limitations once you figure the best route to the score. I wouldn't be surprised if someone has written down a list of moves to get there, but I'd hate to ruin the fun by looking that up, and it would be boring for me to sit down and optimize via the building book and all that. I'd rather just set up the game, and play from what I know and see how it goes for the fun of it. Whether I win or lose playing solo with this game makes a little difference, but isn't as important as playing it for fun. It is a complex box of delights for sure.

RATING: 9
Played first game last night, three players, "wine and bread" version.

First impression - wow!  It's like two of my favorite games, Le Havre and Agricola, had a beautiful child. ;-)

The resource dial works much better than Le Havre's ever-growing piles of tokens, and unlike Le Havre, you need to keep getting *some* basic materials up through the end of the game.

I both love, and find incredibly frustrating, the bushiness of the decision tree.  Lots of ways to go, but you always feel like maybe there was something better.

The only down side, for a first game, was not really having a good idea of what all the buildings did, and constantly looking over other players mats to see what options were available.

All in all, a great game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I think of Ora et Labora as "Improved Le Havre." It has a superior technology for allocating the available resources. It has an enjoyable spatial element that is missing in Le Havre. It has more interesting timing decisions because of the three pawns, and because of the need to clear land. It is a harder game to master than Le Havre. And finally and most importantly, it has a broader array of strategies and tactics to pursue.

In Le Havre, there were a variety of ways to approach the early game. But by the latter half of the game, they all will have evolved into a nearly identical endgame. Ora et Labora gives the players many more options than Le Havre, and this remains true all the way to the end of the game. For this reason, Ora et Labora seems to offer more variety from game to game. You would think it should offer less variety, since the setup is exactly the same every time, while Le Havre features some minor but important randomness in the setup. But that hasn't been my experience. The wealth of viable options in Ora et Labora creates different patterns and different challenges each time.

RATING: 9

RATING: 10
Less obviously brutal than Agricola, but probably on par with Le Havre. I like the tech tree mechanic of sorts, where certain buildings definitely work with certain future buildings. I've yet to get a good grasp of the strategy, though I think I'm getting better at realizing the benefits of forcing others to place their workers rather than going pseudo-solitaire.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A lighter version of agricola with some of the same ideas.  It is a resource management timing game where you are building an economic engine to create victory points through variable cards that come up for purchase.  The record keeping on the resources is taken care of with the wheel you see in photos of the game, which is a lot less fiddly than adding pieces to the piles in agricola.  

It has the same "grab it while the stack is tall" system for obtaining resources that you saw in agricola.  Its combined with a ton of variable cards which you add to the basic rooms to create a VP economy (hopefully).  My first game I managed to get a mill and a brewery.  A few lucky high wheat turns later and I was cranking out tons of beer which I used to finance a lot of expensive purchases later, even though I never really took a card that transformed the beer into victory points, I still came in a close second place by just using the beer to buy all kinds of different victory point related stuff that was available like settlements.  Another player built a building that turned fuel into victory points and a peat roaster that turned peat into coal.  That was a nice engine for him but he ended up building a wonder late that I think was a waste of his resources.  Overall this part of the game was very fun and very interesting to me.  It reminds me a lot of the fun part of agricola, only without nearly as many of the "how do I do X" moments.

It also unfortunately has almost no interaction, which was my biggest gripe about agricola and is my biggest gripe about this game.  You compete somewhat for the cards in the marketplace, but there isn't really much of an effect on any other player's position.  This criticism of the game should be taken with a grain of salt, because we did not take advantage of the fact that you can use other people's workers.  We were so caught up in other things no one did it.

I'm also not in love with the fact that card placement in your tableau makes for so many points, when it doesn't really feel like that is part of the economic engine building that is the strength of the game.  I suspect that little gripe was the result of only playing once and that in future games, I will understand whats going on with the strategic placement of the cards a little better and enjoy it more.

RATING: 5
This is a bad game for me, because there are so many cards to learn about that my brain ends up getting lost in the forest of possibilities.  It's worse than Le Havre and even Caylus in that respect -- each building breaks the rules in a different way, and costs a different set of resources, so there are way too many things to consider.  It's even worse given that you can use other players' buildings for one gold.  That makes it impossible to play simultaneously -- you have to wait until the previous player has decided what building they will use (or whether they will use your last pawn so that you can't use it at all, which can really wreak havoc on your plans).

I also dislike the fact that a winning score is about 200 points -- it's hard for me to grasp scores that big.

I think this is a game without randomness, much like Caylus... but I don't think that's a good thing.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
If you like Rosenberg's heavier gamer offerings, this should appeal to you. Me, I often find them just a tad bit on the complex side for my taste. There's a lot to take in here, and it all seems balanced at first blush, so your choices are myriad. There are several ways to score, and at least 8 types of goods that you'll have to use as currency for buildings, settlements, and other goods and VP items. Finding the combos and coming up with a decent engine will require some analysis.

The big wheel is the central mechanism, and it works very well in the good old Puerto Rico way-- choices which are left unselected become more and more valuable until someone can no longer resist. Like similar mechanics in games past, it's a very clever way to add tension to a game. Should you get that pile of stone now or wait a turn for a bigger pile, taking the risk of someone else grabbing it first? I wonder if a simpler, more elegant game could be made from this one basic idea. Hmm...

As for the rest of the game, buy up lots of special action buildings, put your workers on them, collect resources to trade for other resources, to build more buildings and special scoring areas, and so on. You can also take opponents' actions and waste their workers for a pittance... this can really hose their plans, and was the weakest part of the game for me. I'm sure it was added as a nod to player interaction, but I felt the game would have been fine leaving players to run their own little engines without outside interference. Still, it's a little bit friendlier than Agricola... despite its food and fuel gathering, there is no punitive phase where you must pay up or face consequences. There are special building phases you'll want to be ready for, but they aren't nearly as common and not building every time won't kill your game.

Overall, not a bad game, but I would have been happy with a much more paired down design. The wheel mechanism is great, but really just a new face on an old idea, and the rest of it we've seen elsewhere. For those players that want to plumb its depths, I suspect Ora & Labora may have quite a bit of replayability. As for me, I'll play it on occasion until it loses its luster around here, which I suspect won't take long.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a very impressive game, which take Rosenberg's (2) biggest triumphs, blends the best flavours, and comes up with a pleasant confection indeed.

Ora & Labora (O&L) has the tableau building and rural themes of Agricola, but the (slightly) kinder goods accumulation and availability of Le Havre, and has a feel of constructive building more like the latter game on a 1st impression.

Like Agricola and Le Havre it has very specific cards to better suit play for various numbers of gamers, well-written and complete rules (though sometimes a little hard to find specific rules, they were generally clear enough for me to recall having read a rule... now where was it again?), and nice graphics which delineate the play.  It has user-specific builds, timing issues, and resource collection vs. other critical action conflicts.

O&L does not have a mandatory chow call, which I think is a nice change.  The theme of medieval life still carries though pretty well.

The boards/sheets are flimsier than in previous games, but it also packs better into the box, and seems lighter than predecessor games with more "cardboard/wood goodness".

On the whole, the game play was very satisfying.  Like Agricola and Le Havre on the 1st play I made many rules mistakes, but generally caught them by the end of the game.

I think the play flowed quite well, and had a "kinder, gentler" feel which suited the monks' non-agressive/acquisitive stance.

It was hard to give an initial rating, but I LIKED this game more than Agricola or Le Havre; I had to rate it better than either, so it starts at an 8 on a 1st play, with room to slide or to climb from there.

I definitely look forward to more plays of this, soon!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game offers a very good combination of spatial development and process management. Most victory points will usually be made with the buildings and settlement tiles in your fief. Especially the settlements, scoring only horizontally and vertically adjacent buildings, need careful planning. As a third layer, one also needs to focus on ressources and production chains for goods and victory point tiles. Due to the latter ORA ET LABORA is similar to LE HAVRE, due to the first it feels related to AGRICOLA. A combination I like a lot. Playing time easily exceeds 2 hours but is worth every minute spent if you are looking for cleverly interwoven development and ressource management mechanisms.

Although you mainly plan your own development, interaction with other players is nicely integrated not only with timing issues for ressource collection and aquisitions of buildings. One can also pay other players to use one of their pawns in one of their buildings for one's own benefit. Neat little needle-picks here.

RATING: 4
