[ENG]

Resources managment, planning the placement of buildings and how to use them, worker placement with a setting about medieval monastery of Europe.

If Le Havre had married Farmers of the Moor, Ora et Labora would have been be their son because is similar to both. It polish some aspects of their desings doing a more open-ending game with a lot of options. Outstandings detalis about it desing are: the rondel (wheel) to manage resources in the board (it eliminates the knotty task of resource replacement), adds the spatial situation when you build (now you´ve more things to do, not only what to build but where you ´ll constuct it). Goods are not useless at the final game (yes; I´m thinking on Le Havre´s clay and Agricola´s wood). Finally, it also includes two different scenarios: Ireland and France.

There´s no luck except the first player draw, but I don´t know if this is good or bad, becouse it could happen you´ll play several identical games. CONS AP+, extremely little cards. Price. Real expensive game.

RATING: 8
The most similar game from the Rosenberg's portfolio would be Le Havre.

It's generally good if you need several plays to get a good control over the game.  But here it almost crosses the line.  After 10+ plays I _still_ have a feeling that I need to play OeL at least once week to be any good at it...
There are so many possibilities and things to do here that prioritization, setting and following a strict path are extremely hard.  Fully conscious play is difficult.  Usually you want to do too much.

To sum up: Rosenberg in good, old shape, but don't expect anything revolutionary here.  Rondel is merely a technical solution and spacing of buildings / using opponents' workers are nice, but not ground breaking concepts.

Rating will go up ONLY if I achieve better control over my play.

Still, all above remarks aside, Agricola and Le Havre seem better...

--

3-player mode is significantly better than 4-player one.  More actions (you can achieve more) and more control. 
 And don't try 2-player mode.

RATING: 7.5
2015-05-29

Initial Rating: 7.5 (May 2015)

Uwe Rosenberg is one of my favorite game designers, so I always expect a lot from his games. Happily, Ora et Labora is a very good gamer's game :).

The game comes with lots and lots of components, sadly, many of them were damaged when I punched them. Also the player boards are very flimsy. I'm thinking about laminating them. 

The rules were easier than I expected but the decisions in the game are far from obvious due to the sheer number of buildings. Each building has a cost and an effect but you also have to take into account where to build it (strong spatial element) and the type of terrain in which you can build it. It it's great :D.

Ora et Labora comes with excellent (and vital) player aids. 

The resource wheel is very well done and does an excellent work tracking the resources and the game rounds. I think this was the first game in which Rosenberg used it but I already played other games with it (Le Havre: The Inland Port and Glass Road).

It comes with two variants (France and Ireland) that have different resources and cards. Very neat. So far I can't really compare them because I have only played Ireland. 

I was worried that the static setup could lead to a low replay value, but my gut feeling is telling me each game will be different. The are just too many options with the buildings.

I'm indifferent towards the theme, but I have to say many buildings feel very thematic. 

On the bad side, I think the cards are too small so you can't see them clearly across the table. Also, it came with lots of manuals (I think four?) and as I read the rules almost exclusively on the iPad I couldn't find all of them. However, this is a very minor dislike.

It was very expensive for me, but this week I heard about an upcoming reprint. 

Bottom line, Ora et Labora is a very good resource conversion game. Very reminiscent of both Le Havre (resource conversion) and Le Havre: The Inland Port, but much more closer to the latter. It has a lot of potential and I want to play it again soon.

One of 2011`s favorites.

Current Rating:  7.5
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Ora et Labora is largely a reimagining of Le Havre.  That’s not a bad thing.  Le Havre is a great game.

The game flow is similar.  Each player gets one action per turn, with the start player getting a second action.  Since each player is start player an equal number of times there are an equal number of actions per player, with each player getting a fair chance at the greater basic item offers.

Even though the game flows similarly there are major differences which make Ora et Labora more than simply another version of the same game.  Players build buildings on their own game board, and the spatial arrangement is important.  One of the major sources of victory points at game end is settlement value.  Each building has both an intrinsic building value and a settlement value; the settlement value only scores if the building is next to a settlement.  Some buildings are more desirable to have near settlements - vineyards are pleasant to be next to and have high settlement values, but no one wants to be near the slaughterhouse.

The goods conversions are more streamlined; Ora et Labora eliminates the Shipping Line from Le Havre, instead making several goods intrinsically worth points. Another aspect different from Le Havre is the workers.  While in Le Havre each player has one worker who moves from building to building, in Ora the players have 2-3 workers who work only their buildings.  Opponents can donate coins to get other players’ workers to work opponents’ buildings for them - or later in the game, they can ply the opposing monks with whiskey or wine instead.  When at the beginning of a turn all workers are spent, they return to the player’s supply for future use.  In addition the prior is a special worker who can activate a building immediately after it has been built; it is important to make the most of these bonus actions.

Where Ora really distinguishes itself is the spatial aspect.  Settlements serve as the upkeep of the game; the penalty for not having enough food and fuel is to miss out on these important VP opportunities.  Arranging one’s buildings optimally can be an engrossing puzzle for the first few games, irrespective of what other players are doing.

Adding to replayability is that there are two different building sets and slightly different goods, corresponding to France and Ireland themes.  The game has obvious expansion possibilities in new goods tiles and building cards for different country themes - though I did like Agricola Farmers of the Moor it’s nice to see that this game can be expanded without an additional layer of rules complexity.

Ora et Labora might be the equal or even superior of Uwe Rosenberg’s two top 10 entries Agricola and Le Havre.   I plan on playing 20+ times to find out.

RATING: 7
Ora et Labora is the true third part of the harvest trilogy. There are two notable differences to Agricola/Le Havre. First, there's no single random element (no cards). Second, due to the clever turn wheel there's no need to refill all resources every turn. Still, it's a three hour game. 
The biggest problem of Ora et Labora is it's late birth. While I enjoyed it as much as Le Havre, it's basically more of the same and I'm not playing enough Agricola or Le Havre to warrant a purchase. In fact, Ora et Labora brought me to downgrading Le Havre to a 7 as I've recognized that due to their length and lots of competition in their niche I never will play these games enough to get the full potential out of them and on an occasional play too much is lost to rate them higher.

RATING: 4
Surprised this is so popular. I like heavy games, but this game makes me really appreciate Agricola, which gives very fixed point rewards for goals (1 grain? 1 point. 4 grain? 2 points). Here, everything is worth points and the possibilities are overwhelming. Very subject to AP.

RATING: 6
Alcoholic monks play at shuffling commodities around the port of Le Havre, except it's really a bit of countryside with a spatial element (for the locations, not the commodities). The game feels less intuitive and more fussy, at least to a newcomer. The need to coordinate your building placements and available resources for each settlement phase (really an inverted feeding phase) as well as for the final building opportunities means that good play involves planning and re-planning your boards and actions early, often and very carefully. My first game was plagued by false options, lack of overview and beginner mistakes, so further play is needed for proper evaluation. I suppose some of the elements that felt awkward, like keeping track of available options on other player boards, quickly get better with experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
After one play, anyway, this is not really my thing. I might play it occasionally in the future, but I doubt I'll buy it unless I have a real change of heart.

Here's the deal. This game suffers not from a lack of player aids, but from a lack of the right kind of player aids.

Imagine a game where a red cube and a blue cube combine to make a purple cube. You need purple cubes to win. Great! You've just played Fresco.

Now imagine a game where you have fish that you've caught and you have to flip to the Smoked Herring side in order to eat them. Great! You've just played Le Havre.

Now imagine you have seven or eight goods, they can all get flipped (one without even taking an action... just don't flip it back) but in order to do so you have to combine good A with good B, sometimes with good E to get good F or to get 30 victory points. You can also combine good A with good D to get good G (which is really good E, flipped). And so on. Congratulations! You've played Ora et Labora.

This isn't a linear set of conversions, it's a web. The web doesn't appear as such on the player aid. For numbskulls such as myself, Ora et Labora is just way too unintuitive. It feels like at the end of the day, you could just be trading up cubes in a long chain from red through green, yellow, purple, puce, mauve, burnt umber, and tangerine all the way through to the holy grail of beige. The sequence can double back on itself and there's nothing as self evident as live fish -> smoked herring or clay -> brick.

I really enjoyed the building placement aspect, which I gamed carefully enough to fall just four points short of the ultimate victor. It didn't take a whole lot. Mostly I just grabbed goods when there were plenty, bought expensive buildings, and made a long chain of settlements. That was straightforward. But the production end of the game - which is where you score the really serious veeps - completely lost me somewhere between peat and whisky.

It's fussy, and yet open-ended. And while open-endedness is usually something I admire in a game, in Ora, there are so many options that it's really hard to know what to do. This might resolve itself over several plays, but given the complexity of the web and its interactions with the dozens of buildings involved, I'm not sure I have it in me to invest the time. 

The game comes with two very different games in the box - you can play with France or with Ireland, and how cool is that? Expansions could add even more - you could spend a bunch of grain to grab a handful of buildings that only you could build, or you could build a thieves' guild that allows you to snatch stone from a neighbor. I mean, the possibilities are endless. But they could involve even less intuitive relationships, in which case, no thanks.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Fun game.  I like it and really want to play it again.  I see the similarities to Le Havre but this game seems a bit sloppier.  Although I might just prefer the theme of this one over Le Havre's, I think Le Havre is the better game.

Unfortunately, this game doesn’t improve on the one logistical complaint I have with Le Havre (the difficulty in reading/seeing the buildings of other players).  In Le Havre I can mitigate this by having a central common building display area with colored markers to indicate who owns the building.  This fix is impossible in Ora because the spatial positioning of buildings on each separate player board is critical to game play.

The resource wheel is a neat mechanism.  It's duplicated (twice) in the upcoming Glass Road from the same designer.

This game takes just as long to play as Le Havre.  Anyone thinking this was going to be more of the same but with a shorter play time will be disappointed.

Finally, there has been some debate about the component quality.  I only recognized this with the thickness of the player boards and extensions.  Yes they are thin and it would be nicer if they were thicker.  However, it is not really noticed during game play.

Overall, I like this game and definitely want to play it more.  But I think Le Havre is better, followed by Ora, with Agricola a distant third.  But for now, I'll forgo purchasing it (due to the spatial concern) until I can try Glass Road and Caverna (okay, done; if I could find this at a decent price I would buy it).

RATING: N/A
For me this is tough to rate after one game.
Basicly it is resource worker placement conversion games with some tqbleau building You trade resources for buildings and these buildings give you resources 

Now I think it is a 7 . Both friends I have played this with think highly of it.
I give it a bit more because of the tableau building.

I am not sure if this is 4 player game.
The three player game was perfect. We all had 33 actions. In the four player game this will be 25 unless I missed something.
Scores were high but not so far apart. I had 234, 208 and 203.
My tableau building won me the game.  I had a 68 point difference with the player who scored the second highest.
There are a ton of points to be made in resource conversion into VP . I only scored 16 points there. The best was 54 but there was one building were the sum of the resources was 40 after conversion.

To round of my thoughts this is an excellent game in the genre but if you own a few you probably own enough.

RATING: 7
Played once, 4p.  Hmmm...  Did not really live up to my (admittedly high) expectations.  I think there may be one layer too many of things going on.  Too many resources, too many ways to exchange resources, too many ways to convert to victory points, and too many different buildings to keep track of.  It is also possible to completely screw somebody accidentally (forcing them to use their last worker, leaving them with nothing remotely useful to do, essentially giving them a big victory point hit) - not sure what the point of that is.  At this point, Le Havre is still the preferred game, but I LOVE the resource wheel mechanism.  I think the game is maybe a bit too fiddly, and scaling back the fiddliness but keeping the resource wheel would make for a great game.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
A long (3h+) deep euro in the purest Rosenberg tradition.  Mechanically it feels a lot like Le Havre without the feeding & ship, but with slightly more meaningful player interactions and a lot more viable options at any given point.  That makes for a more open, slightly more forgiving play which isn't a bad thing for a game this long.  The flow is fairly good and it scales a touch better than Le Havre (which I only ever really play with 3 nowadays).  On the surface, it's also reminiscent of Agricola with the building grids on the player mat, but that's a fairly weak relationship.  All in all it's a solid entry is a genre that's already fairly crowded.  Fans of the designer will want to own this one too.  Like its siblings, this game has a lot to offer to the right crowd with sufficient time to invest (which is easier said than done).

RATING: 8
Your first game is likely to be as long and painful as your first game of Le Havre...especially if everyone is new.  The cards are small and one can easily just get caught up in doing what is in front of you (which is ideal if the machine is generating points).  I liked the resource rondel which really keeps the resource accounting more simple as to what is available to select.  Mind you, you will still have a ton of chits in front of you to try and bend into points in some fashion.  For once, it was also nice not to have to worry about feeding your people ALTHOUGH you still want to acquire a fair amount of food to build the better settlements if possible.  I also liked the aspect that there are two variants as there is a French variant and an Irish variant where a couple resources change and the buildings act differently.  The player aids were effective but I wasn't impressed in the quality of the paper used - might need to laminate those.  I also would have preferred cardboard terrain as the cardstock tends to warp a little.

RATING: 5
I should start by saying that I really dislike Agricola. Le Havre I like a little better, but the agonizing microsteps of every single action drive me insane. As a result, I wasn't expecting too much from Ora et Labora, since it seemed to be much of the same.

Turns out, it pretty much is. It's a little better in that the basic resources actually are basic resources and not pre-resources you need to convert into actual resources to get anything done. But there's still the same bizarreness that if someone chops down trees before I do, I get fewer of them. 

I'm still not a fan of this stuff, but if I had to play one of the three, I guess it would be this one? It's really not an appealing choice to make.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
After our first play, not sure what to make of it yet. Want to play again.

Our way of approaching any game is to let the game surprise you the first play. No looking through cards before the game. This hampers strategic play, but makes the first game exploratory along the lines of "What does this button do".

I'm not sure whether I like the abundance of choices. I felt AP crawling up my leg. It seems there are several ways to play this. Either tactical (my innate preference) or strategic. Tactical here means just wait and see what is left to do and rate choices in terms of efficiency. strategic means calculating the actions left to you until the next village building fase. I think being able to switch between tactical and strategic is key here.

Strategic placement plays a big role. And that is an interesting addition to the Agricola-like harvest theme. 
Having to buy land to build on, also very nice.

This is probably a game for which I have to be in the right mood. Sometimes I'm just too tired/lazy to think strategically.

RATING: 7
Uwe Rosenberg game where you gather resources to build buildings which provide special abilities.  Building placement is very important as it provides substantial end of game scores.  The game has a number of economic paths to victory.  Monastery buildings provide special abilities that give you benefits in game.  

You can also use other players buildings by paying a gold or or wine or beer.  

Overall, the spatial element is very interesting and adds an interesting dimension to game play.  

I view this as and excellent game and put it right up with Le Havre, At the Gates of Loyang and Caverna. Agricola is in its own amazing class of greatness.

RATING: 7.5
A solid effort from Uwe Rosenberg, but not as good as Agricola or Le Havre. Technically, there are far more strategic pathways to take in this game, and the incremental improvement over Le Havre with the inclusion of the resource wheel, plus the return of tile placement from Agricola should all make for a great game, but something's missing. It could be that there are so many ways to convert different resources that it never really feels like you get an efficient engine going... the final turns of Ora et Labora seem to lack the feeling of things finally falling into place that made Agricola and Le Havre so satisfying. Of course, that could just be my inexperience with the game talking; time will tell.

RATING: 7
[u]Pros:[/u]
[b]Simple, straightforward turns[/b]
[b]Production wheel[/b] to determine amount of goods = excellent (except when you can't remember whether you turned the wheel at the right time)
[b]Two variants![/b] (I smell easy expansions - England, Spain, perchance?)
[b]Pay-for-work[/b] to use other player's buildings
[b]Well-balanced[/b] for two

[u]Cons:[/u]
[b]Components.[/b] Some of my chits ripped, boards too thin, cards smaller than I'd like.
[b]Rulebook(s).[/b] They needed a simpler way to explain this game.  I was totally turned off by this and had to watch a video on BGG to learn how to play!  Rulebook focuses on 3-4 players, which is frustrating when you almost always play with two.
[b]Complexity.[/b]  More options per turn than Agricola, and the complexity increases over the course of the game (this could be a pro depending on what you like in your games).


I've only played a couple games, but I've had fun with this so far and think it's actually a very good game.  It's so complex, though, that I don't think I'll get to play it as often as I like (my husband doesn't like the expanding complexity).

[i]Sold, as Le Havre does a better job at everything this game tries to do.  Also it has bigger cards![/i]

Once you get going though it starts making sense -- I wouldn't call it easy but it's certainly manageable. Just get a good night's sleep before playing.

Also, it's not just multi-player solitaire as you'll definitely want to [i]try[/i] to keep track of what your opponents plans may be. They can place in your buildings and of course build things that you've been saving up for.

Everything I love about Uwe's previous farming games is found in this.A 10!

RATING: 7
Feels more open than Le Havre, which always feels like shipping steel is the one optimal strategy. Gone are the feeding requirements - no upkeep in this game. That's an aspect I greatly miss, it exerts pressure. Instead there are settlement phases where you have the opportunity to place special buildings for bonus points. If you miss out on this, you fall behind. There are some complaints of the euro points grab where there are VP everywhere, which is valid. The interaction is minor, but it's there and it can be important as you can force your opponent to use their workers.

All in all, it's an optimization game and a fine one.

RATING: 7.5


Wow, only one play, but wow - both my wife and I really enjoyed this to the point where we discussed the game in great detail the next day and (even better) she may be willing to lift the boardgame purchase embargo.

I like how this game plays and how it looks - great production values. When I saw the resource pieces were double sided I was worried that this would be something like Le Havre, which I find to be fiddly. In practice this was not the case and I could keep track of what was what pretty easily; as could Mrs Fish who has not yet had the pleasure of playing Le Havre.

The dial that determines resource availability is very good and forces some really interesting decisions on the players and the 'need to feed' is not a pronounced as either Le Havre or Agricola both of which are pretty good change-ups.

So, nothing to dislike about this one so far and, did I mention, Mrs Fish likes it so should I purchase it (Cancon, probably) then it will get a fair bit of table time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
This is not as tight as Agricola or Le Havre, so it feels much more relaxed.  There is no stress of having to feed people. What's more, being blocked from an expected action doesn't lead to that crushing feeling of failure that you can get in Agricola or Le Havre; there is always an expansive array of options at your disposal.  Every turn feels overwhelming and fascinating.

However, the game doesn't have a great arc:  even though my tableau grows, I don't actually feel the sense of growth that you get in Le Havre (where you ship for 100+ points in the final rounds).   In the final rounds of Ora et Labora, one action can get you a Wonder or 3 relics (representing a lot of points), but you doesn't *feel* like a dramatic change over what you accomplish in the first rounds.

RATING: 9.5
A game of efficiency. Like Le Havre, you are presented with 10+ types of goods to build things, make food and transform them to goods with better value. The turn structure is similar - you take goods or use/build a building. 

One key difference between OeL and Le Havre is the addition of spatial placement in OeL, which adds a little complexity. On the flip side, the placement of settlement is effectly a feeding round as it grants points to players that are prepared. (as opposed to deducting points for not able to prepare enough foods)

Despite the randomized special building in Le Havre, it seems that there's a little more variety in OeL, as there are more routes to victories.

With two games almost identical, Le Havre seems to be more elegant and streamlined than OeL. Still, Both games are fantastic!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In France/Ireland, players fell trees or cut peat, build buildings, or send their clergymen to work in buildings. The central mechanism is a wheel that turns every round, and players gather their resources depending where the good is located on the wheel. The player mat has some peat and trees that have to be removed before buildings can be placed (much like Farmers of the Moor). 

Everytime the wheel passes a settlement phase, players build a settlement, which score points for adjacent buildings. Otherwise, VPs, are scored for buildings and goods (like Le Havre).

There are a large number of buildings and decisons so it is hard to find the optimal path. I enjoy trying to solve these sorts of problems and look forward to more plays. Rating likely to rise after more plays.

RATING: 9
If you love Agricola or Le Havre, most likely you'll love Ora et Labora as well. The complexity is a little bit above Le Havre. But there is no dealing with debt or hunger though, makes the game felt "less pressuring". 

If you love Glen More, this game might be suitable for you. Ora et Labora is almost similar to Glen More, complete with whiskey, and even more, with beer and wine! 

And, if you love Homesteaders, then this game might be for you too. Ora et Labora is almost similar to Homesteaders but without the bidding mechanic. Collecting resources from buildings, building building-combo, and building high-VP-building are the main activities in both games. 

I rated it lower than Agricola, because Agricola can be played faster, and the playing strategy is easier to explain to newbies.

RATING: 7
A good game, but perhaps overly complicated by the myriad of different available resources. I feel like it could have been narrowed down to fewer of them, as to avoid some of the inevitable analysis paralysis that a game like this causes.

The resource wheel is a very good idea, which makes managing the value of the different resources a breeze. Unfortunately the overall quality of the components is not quite good, and all the expansion boards end up being bent one way or another, since they are so thing.

Given how much of a brain burner it can ben, I'm not sure I would rather play this one, instead of other german titles, but it is a good game nonetheless.

RATING: 8.5
A great 'early Uwe' game. Interesting mechanics, indirect meddling with your partner's plans. The only thing I do not like about the 2 player version is the absence of a fixed end. Because of that the game can easily drag for quite some time and overstay its welcome. Other than that, excellent. If you like Agricola + Le Havre, I would recommend at least giving it a shot.

RATING: 7
Nienawidzę gier, których muszę uczyć się na pamięć... to jedna z takich gier.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
[b]Ora et Labora[/b] 

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8/10 -> [b]10[/b]):[/COLOR]

[i]Ora has become one of my son's favorite Uwe games, so it's managed to hit the table quite a bit in the last year given how long it takes to play. A year ago I said I enjoyed Ora more than Le Havre due to the spatial aspect of placing buildings, which I still think is a great mechanic, but I've since reacquired Le Havre and come to the conclusion that it may be my favorite Uwe game at this moment. I can definitely say, without hesitation, that Ora et Labora and Le Havre offer different play experiences and I'm glad to have both in my collection. If I want to play something punishing and tense I'll play Le Havre and if I want a more laid back experience I'll choose Ora et Labora. 

Like all of Uwe's games, Ora plays great with 2 and while it has an involved set up the game flows smoothly once you're playing. My son and I can usually wrap up a game in 90 minutes. Confrontation is low although you are competing to build the same buildings, but similar to Le Havre you can pay to use someone else's building. Another change from Le Havre is your worker pool has two workers and one abott. The abbott is a great addition because when you build a building you can immediately send him to the building to activate it; this sets up the opportunity to create some very productive turns. 

All in all Ora et Labora is yet another Uwe game I love and would easily recommend to folks that enjoy a meaty resource management/conversion game that offers a unique spatial aspect to the game and some very different ways to score points [i](i.e. settlements vs. wonders)[/i]. With the recent reprint Ora et Labora is now easy to find and definitely worth checking out.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (10):[/COLOR]

[i]This was the last of Uwe's heavier games I'd yet to play. After two plays it probably lands in my top three Uwe games (alongside Agricola and At the Gates of Loyang, bumping out Glass Road). Ora shares many similarities to Le Havre (think lots of goods chits that can be flipped over to upgrade them). I enjoy Ora a lot more because of the spacial aspect to the building layout on your board can lead to a lot of end game points if you plan ahead properly. It was also interesting to see how Uwe took the resource wheel of Ora and re-implemented/streamlined it in Glass Road. 

I played with 2 and 3 players and it played equally well at both counts. This will definitely see double digit plays over the next year, I'm glad I finally decided to add this one to our collection. Most months Ora et Labora would have been my game of the month, but GoL was that good.[/i]
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Rating 9 after one 2p game.

Excellent game with a ton of options and paths to follow. Those carping on lack of replayability are probably getting ahead of themselves - I don't think there's a single "killer" strategy that will always work, and the timing issues of how to get such a strategy to work given that the buildings you need will likely be spread across several players will probably be enough to neuter it even if it does exist.

The potential for expansion of the system is just as wide as with Agricola or Le Havre, the only difference is that you might have to wait for another card set to come out for a while. But until then, I can easily see investing a few dozen plays into both the French and Irish versions, at which point any reasonable person would have to admit they got their money's worth.

RATING: 8
規則:
*根據人數選用資源圖版，在用指針正面(背面為兩人遊戲使用)，根據人數跟國家選擇使用的建築物卡牌分成ABCD四疊並放在對應位置，石頭跟葡萄以外資源放在資源圖版，指針指向一，起始建築卡打開，建築token放在A當作提醒，地景版塊按金額最低的在上面疊起放旁邊
*每個玩家拿家園圖版上面放兩沼澤在右跟三森林跟三個修士還有對應的駐地卡，起始各基礎資源一個共六個(黏土木材泥媒金幣麥牲畜)
*決定起始玩家後把用對應國家的起始標記翻到一枚金幣那面給玩家，每輪分成五個階段
A 如果全部三個修士都在圖版上，就全部拿回手
B 旋轉生產輪一格，法國時葡萄在第八輪進場，石頭在13輪進場
C 指針轉過小房子標記時進入駐地階段，結束後開新的房子卡進場
*第五次跟最後一次駐地階段發生在遊戲結束時
*駐地階段 > 移動小房子到下個階段 > 每個玩家可以蓋一個駐地 > 分發這個階段的新駐地給每位玩家
D 每個玩家從起始玩家開始執行一個動作，起始玩家在大家做完後可再做一個動作，動作有三個選擇
1.放一個工人到圖版上建築物執行，可花一元請別人的放在別人的建築物，可放了不執行
*特定建築物蓋好後使用別人工人變成兩元，起始玩家標記翻面
*可不付錢改付酒或威士忌到供應區來用別人工人
*拿資源則看資源輪有幾個拿完歸零，可使用方塊joker來代表各種資源
2.清森林拿木頭或清沼澤拿泥煤，資源量也看資源輪，沒有還是可以移牌不拿資源
3.支付左上角資源蓋一個建築物在自己圖版對應地形上，黃色建築必須相連，蓋玩建築物後可馬上使用主教修士使用該建築物
*額外行動 可作多次 
1.翻麥成稻草，但是不能翻回來
2.一元可換五元，酒跟威士忌可換對應錢
3.買地景 一回合一次，駐地階段也可買一次，地景放置時價格要在右下角，森林卡放在森林位置，沼澤卡也放在對應位置
E 起始玩家順時鐘換人
*3-4人在25輪E階段時先拿回主教並獲得一個額外行動(可放主教到任意建築或蓋建築)
*計分 
1.物品上的黃色分數加總
2.建築物駐地分數加總
3.駐地相鄰分數加總(紅色駐地分數跟相鄰的建築紅色分數)
****短版***
*使用對應的資源輪跟生產指針，起始版塊少一森林跟沼澤，遊戲進行12輪+額外回合，3人使用兩人建築物，4人使用三人建築物，每個人少一個基礎修士
1.每次有人生產輪拿資源，每個人都拿到一個，包括生產的人
2.每輪也會收到指針指到的兩個貨物
***兩人****
*使用對應生產輪跟建築物，指針使用背面
*遊戲改為輪流行動每次可作兩個動作，作完指針就前進
*工人在一開始時如果三個用完就回手，結束時用完也回手
*沒有額外回合
*遊戲進行到D階段後當場上剩下一間房子時，下個玩家最後一個行動後結束
***Solo***
*使用對應資源圖版跟正面指針，對應建築物
*地景版塊價格改成高到低
*葡萄跟石頭不使用，百搭方塊放在A起始
*起始無資源，每輪作兩個動作
*當指針到10還有物品在前面時 移出遊戲
*中立玩家 沒森林跟沼澤，有起始建築市場在左上角，工人是給你付錢雇的，也可用酒雇
*駐地階段時，所有剩下的建築給中立玩家，建築可以任意覆蓋，除了黃色要給黃色蓋，黃色蓋時也要相鄰，地形限制不管
*中立玩家蓋完，你可以馬上用他的主修士 使用其建築物，仍要付錢
*每輪看是不是三個可拿回手
*最後一輪是先做完在幫中立玩家蓋，其他時候都是先蓋完，在輪到自己行動

祈禱與耕作

RATING: 8.5
Lovely game, my first "euro" purchase in many, many years. I wanted a game that hit on some of the "best" euro ideas of the past few years and this one showed up on several lists. Good purchase, expensive for what you get IMO. Feels more like a ... $50ish purchase, not a $70. Although, given the fact there are "Ireland" and "France" versions, it's practically like getting two games in one box. I don't regret spending the $70.

This game feels like a lot of other games when I play it. Mostly it feels to me like the Settlers card game, in three dimensions while spun up on speed. You're attaching locations to locations, juggling a dozen different resources, chasing VPs along several tracks (goods, locations and settlements). The economies lock together in baroque ways, and I think the game only gets strategic once all your players know wtf is even in the locations deck.

The complete lack of randomness feels scary but I've never had two games play the same. The rondel mechanic is wonderful -- my first rondel game! Very clever. 

Things I'm not loving so far:

* Really hard to read everyone's locations. This is probably eased a lot when everyone knows the locations. Until then, it's tedious while players go through and systematically review everyone's playmat every turn.

* Huge space suck on the table. This game takes more space than Mage Knight to play!

* Hard to eyeball peoples' current scores. Might be a feature and not a bug. Still deciding.

Anyway, I'm very glad to have gotten my old Euro bug scratched with this game. Got this and Dungeon Lords (and Mage Knight) in about the same time, and while they all have very Euro mechanics, this one feels the most stuffy/NPR. Feel like I should be eating cheese and complaining about America when I play this.

RATING: 8
One of those games like Le Havre and Homesteaders where you do better if you study the buildings, know what's coming up and therefore know what resources you need to have to build those buildings exactly when they appear. And then either have a pre-planned strategy, or have studied the game enough to be able to develop and implement a cohesive strategy along the way, on how you're going to convert resources into major points using your buildings' powers. Which all seems like too much work to me to really-really enjoy the game. None-the-less, it's obviously a fine piece of design, providing numerous paths to explore on how to convert resources into VPs. And if that's the type of thing that gets your boat floated, you'll no doubt enjoy this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Ora et Labora is an improved version of Le Havre.  The game removes Le Havre's economic backbone--coins are still present, but the game really revolves around collecting the right resources, of which coins are one of many.  Feeding is replaced by optionally building settlements; these can be paid for with a variety of different resources, and score big at the end of the game.  Resource collection is replaced with a less-fiddly resource wheel, and imbalance of player turns has been eliminated.  Buildings now actually have a physical location, which both restricts their ability to be built as well as affects their end-game scoring.  The result is a deep game that offers a number of paths to victory--something that Le Havre promised but never seemed to quite deliver upon.

(5+ plays)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I noticed this game was made by the creators of Agricola, and since several friends already owned Agricola, I thought I would get this one instead. Instead of a farm, here you are building a monastery. It has a similar mechanic, with workers taking actions and goods stacking up when they are not taken. However, it feels less intuitive than Agricola, with a lot of small pieces and details on the cards adding to a slightly 'messy' feel. Still a perfectly good game in its own right, but doesn't compare all that favourably to its cousin.

RATING: 9
[color=red]Soloable Game[/color]

[color=blue]Playtime: ~90-150 minutes. Depends on the new players as well as player count. Averages about 2 hours.[/color]

Comment: Not sure how I feel about this game. There is a lot I love about it, but some that I feel could be done better. It's a different beast than Agricola and La Havre. There is no pressure for having food/energy although having more of both of those at certain times can give you more points. The biggest complaint I have against this game is that there is zero randomness. You could play 2 exactly the same games if you wanted. This leads me to believe that you may run into group think with this game faster than Agricola or La Havre.

[b]Update[/b]: Alright, when playing with the actual rules, this game is freaking brilliant. We were playing mostly correctly before, but stone came out a full rotation too early and we had screwed up the settlement phase by actually being able to build the building you were handed in that round. Holy hell does that game become much more intense and awesome. I still do feel like it might become slightly scripted, but not having stone early on really changes the whole dynamic of the game.

[b]Update 2:[/b] After finally getting to play this 4 player, I have to say it was great. I still think 3 is the optimal number, but 4 players was much more difficult and the resources were a lot harder to come by. Different buildings, players fighting over things, it was glorious. A bit hard to keep track of everything since the cards are so small, but I liked it a lot. I'm not as fond of the 2 player ending condition as I find it can just drag a bit and you're forced to buy buildings that may be suboptimal just to stop your opponent from doing things. It just feels...lackluster. I enjoy it, but not nearly as much as I do with a higher player count.

[color=green]Optimal Player Count: 3 > 4 >> 2
Works well with all player counts.[/color]

Includes the upgraded player and landscape tiles. They're thick cardboard instead of the thin cardstock they had before.

RATING: 7
Hideous rulebook, I can't mobilize myself into reading this again for a refresher of the rules. The urge to play it again is not strong enough.

Traded away at Alphaspel.

***

Hm, "Ora" gave a pretty good first impression. It was my first game of a Uwe Rosenberg-design as well. But although I had a pleasant time, I still felt slightly underwhelmed.

So, um, exactly how many times do you have to play this game before it starts to feel tense? There are many (too many?) ways to score your precious vp:s. If you can't do this and that just do that and this this round. Rack em up, one way or another - the points will come. 

Of course - one can optimize. Get more points next time, when you are exporing new combos. But when the game generates ridiculous amounts of points for each player at the end, playing good will be more of a "maximizing your points"-thing than "beating your opponents". Many Euros are unfairly accused of being a multiplayer solitarie, but Ora et Labora truly is one.

That beeing said, I actually enjoyed my first game with my wife. The kids saw a film. We where playing and chatting a bit. Drinking some belgian beer. More plays will show exactly what the game brought to that pleasant time.

Oh, one more thing - I really liked the production wheel!

RATING: 6
Pretty much exactly the same thing as Le Havre, and I say that without cynicism. Virtually the only difference is a minor mechanical one that he probably thought of while Le Havre was being shipped and said, "Damn, wish I'd put that in there! Well, might as well just make a new game now." There is an element of spacial positioning, but it's just another layer on top of the cake of sameness, and with the same gameplay as Le Havre comes the same shortcomings: too many options for player turns, too much resource recycling, and too much having to stand up and hump the table so you can see exactly what buildings everybody else has so you can decide what you want to do. He may have (slightly) improved resource handling, but dissemination of player information has still completely eluded his grasp.

A very solid Rosenberg game.

Even though the game is exactly the same during set-up, minor decisions quickly change the resource availability of the game (much like Caylus). 
The added area-management aspect really adds a quirky kind of tension.

Still, though-- a solid resource euro, with the elegant touch of the rondel mechanic.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Whew! This is a complicated one. So many resources that you can turn into other resources in order to turn them into yet other resources to turn them into points... It gets easier after more plays, when you can mentally put all those resources in their separate categories and have some understanding of what they're for and how many you need. It's also easier if you play it two-player, or the three-player short game, as only the basic building will be used, and those have pretty clear uses. The buildings for more players will do things like change energy into money, and it isn't at all obvious whether money is of more use than energy. And having to remember that you have the option of changing your energy into money is a lot more mentally taxing than remembering that you can change malt into beer or cattle into meat.

There's so much to consider before you can take the simple action of picking up a card and placing it on your mat. Do I have the resources to build it? Can I use it immediately? Do I have the resources to use it? Do I have a use for the resources it gives me? Is is worth enough points? Do I have a free space to build it, and if I have multiple, what's the best one? So maybe you want one of the other 10 or so buildings on offer, where you have to ask yourself the same questions...

The beauty of this game is its plethora of strategies, that seem really well balanced. I played Ora & Labora against myself a few times to get a better understanding of how the game worked. Since they only difference between the players was their strategy, I was also interested to find if any one was dominant. The scores were 201/202/206 in my three-player short game, and 352/353 in my two-player game. That's a small sample size, sure, but to me it shows that you really can take many different paths playing Ora, and a difference in scores will be mainly due to skill, and not because of taking a better path.

That makes Ora a very interesting game. The setup and the buildings used are always the same, but there's a lot to explore. Normally, I'm really not a fan of games with multiple paths to victory and multiple ways to score points, as they often feel like playing a spreadsheet instead of other players, but I'll make an exception for Ora. Timing, availability of resources, competition with your fellow players for buildings... a lot of things make you reconsider what you're doing many times during the course of a game. That makes playing it very engaging, and seeing how it all unfolds a really interesting experience.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
Doesn't disappoint. Improves upon Le Havre, wherein the loan-fuelled coal-to-coke-to shipping line strategy is dominant. This is a real sandbox game which, though light on interaction, is incredibly engaging for the number of fantastic options the game presents you with. Like most Uwe games, your actions are your most precious resource, meaning the game can be prone to slight AP. The spatial element is simple but genius, adding another dimension to your engine game. The worker rhythm is another enjoyable mechanic, giving even more importance to proper timing. The game is so deep and complex, I'm convinced only a computer could play it perfectly, and I have no worries about replayability.

RATING: 8
This is a very good game that is similar to Le Havre in building and use of buildings and similar to Farmers of the Moor Agricola expansion in having to clear moor and forest for resources and creation of development spaces.

There is a short version and long version for the 2, 3 and 4 player games, plus a solo game.  Each can be played with either the French cards or the Irish cards.

For your first game, you need to play the short version.  Although the game mechanics are straight forward, there is a lot of complexity in deciding which buildings to build and what to produce.  Your first short 1 hour game is likely to take 3 hours or more.

An excellent mind taxing game. (Not for people who like simple games.)

RATING: 6
+nice theme
+various viable strategies which are fun to create
+nice rondel with available resources

-low replayability
-learning curve is hard and boring (one whole game is a necessary sacrifice)

This game has a lot of planning ahead with a strong, take what you need/want vs take what is very good, element. Placing buildings and terrains is crutial and adds another great aspect to the game. Nice tokens and good theme are a strong part of this game as well.

First game is a nightmare though. Its impossible to plan ahead when you don't know any of like 50 building, 20 resources and witout a clue about placing everything on a table. I strongly suggest a short version for this. Another con is in my opinion replayability below average. There is just a few good strategies on each side (french and irish) and after a few games most of them are exploied, which leaves you with only polishing them, since there is practicaly no way of blocking another player for more than one turn. Buying a crutial building for a strategy is not crutial at all, since you always can buy your way into other player's buildings. 

Overall this is a good game, but it may quickly grow dust on itself after you've played it a lot.

RATING: 8
Of Rosenberg's harvest games I like O&L most. It is an excellent Euro and a real brainburner. In my view the pinnacle of resource/good conversion games.

In comparison to Agricola this is less mean/cutthroat since worker placement is watered down: There is not a common pool of actions, which only the first worker can take and there are a lot more options/buildings, and you can pay other players to use their buildings. But you still have this "should I take 4 clay now or could I wait for 1 round more?". Also you have always the same number of workers, which I like a lot (in Agricola the player being able to get more workers early has a big advantage).
 
[-]The way the games play out offer also more variety than Agricola.[/-][i](I have played Agricola a few times since writing this review and enjoyed Agricola much more. This comment is not true anmyore)[/i] I also like the spatial element of the game, which is more of a nice addition than the central element. The artwork is nice and it adds some fun, when you see your cloister growing. Evem though the cardboard of the player boards is thin, it has a good quality and does not affect gameplay negatively. The cards are a bit tiny, but if they were bigger you would not be able to play this game on a normal table; with reference sheets the small cards are no problem.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.16
I have now played my first game of this (the short three/four player variant). I was impressed by the way the rules were laid out, the intuitive nature of the cards (and the booklet of plain English versions to make sure you knew exactly what each card did). The game seemed less fiddly than Le Havre and yet had a more sophisticated development than Le Havre. Over-all I really enjoyed it. Players take it in turns to take one action, which is mostly a worker placement to activate a building. These generate or convert resources. The basic resources which feed into this are controlled by a rondel, which means that the longer a resources is not gathered the more resources are gained. Then resources are converted to new resources, which can be further converted. Resources can be converted to increase their victory points values or they can then be spent to construct buildings and settlements for more victory points.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Good game, very "thinky" - and with no random elements nor even any variation in setup (except for choosing to play the Ireland or France variant - I've only played twice, both with France), but surely still more than replayable enough due to the immense variety of options available to each player every turn. Not one of my top games to suggest right now, but I'd rarely turn this down - and, more than most games we own, this one will take many plays to become competent in. But, as I said, it's only been played twice since we got it (Christmas 2012), and as more and more new games come out, this one inevitably keeps slipping down the list of ones I want to play. But an excellent game nonetheless, one which I really should try to play more.

RATING: 9
An extended design for the Harvest Triology of Uwe Rosenburg, my favorite designer. The mechanics follow the line of the previous three: place worker to occupy buildings, flip-overs of tokens, food and energy etc. The luck factor is the least of the four, as the buildings are displayed one in a time and the settlements pop out in fixed order. Everything is under control except your opponents' strategies. I especially like the setting where six basic goods are almost of the same value.

Though, two problems stop it reaching the same heights as Le Havre did for me: First, it might possibly have a problem with determinism, especially as a two-player game in the early stages, but I think all games will eventually branch out - which will offset this. Second: it takes hours, longer than Le Havre and Agricola. 

There's enough depth and a solid enough basis to mean even with these problems, I think I'll get my money's worth out of this game - and it's definitely closer to Le Havre than Agricola (which is great for me - as I prefer Le Havre).

I'd say it's a game that should eventually be in every collection, but probably should come after Le Havre.

RATING: 10
This is the zenith of the small economy games that Rosenberg has recently specialized in. I can't imagine this being superseded - it literally contains precisely as many rules, commodities, cards and combos as the cognitive space can handle! Then again - perhaps there are too many commodities. :)

Like all of his games, the economy is very very tight. However, this game excels because it is a bit more difficult to know how to traverse the market - conversions are not as clean as they have been in the past and many times require planning five or six or ten turns in advance. The worst feeling in this game is when you blow a ton of resources on a settlement or a VP cash out, only to realize that you now need to start over. While Agricola is just brutal - O&L is more of an exhilarating race. And while Le Havre is a swift race through a streamlined trade route - this game is a more adventurous one with many sharp turns and diversions.

This is a true masterpiece in its class - I can say confidently after six plays.

RATING: 8.75
Much closer to Le Harve than Agricola.  More deterministic than Le Harve since the buildings always come out in blocks and any building in that block can then be built vs the special buildings + semi random ordering of the buildings in Le Harve.

Excepting the lack of randomization during the setup phase of the game, overall the economy works better than Le Harve and is more balanced with more paths to victory.

Hopefully an expansion adds some way to randomize buildings or some other feature which makes each game a bit different to add make it more re-playable over time.

The game itself is very good, but lack of any randomization and the subpar component quality knock my rating of it down a bit.

RATING: 6.5
Played once with 3P. Ora et Labora is a title which can be justifiably compared to [thing=35677][/thing], both being long, low-luck (or in OeL's case, luck-free) resource conversion games. And I'll reiterate it here; these kind of games, while solidly designed, are not quite to my taste. However, OeL has plenty of unique features of its own, compelling or otherwise.

[b]YAY![/b] Ora et Labora can be played relatively quick, clocking in at 2hrs for a 3P game. Due to the swapping out of the frequent, compulsory feeding phases in Le Havre for the less frequent settlement building phase, OeL [i]feels[/i] a bit more forgiving. Despite the resource dial not being central to how the game plays (unlike in [thing=143693][/thing]), it's a pretty clean mechanism.

[b]HMMM...[/b] The spatial aspect in constructing buildings is an interesting distraction, but it also makes scanning opponents' randomly scattered buildings a rather tedious task. Players familiar with the game shouldn't find this a big issue though.

[b]BOO![/b] The variety of resources borders on being overwhelming, and the resource network doesn't feel as tight as Le Havre's.

For now, I am more enthusiastic playing OeL largely due to its more manageable playing time. It's also interesting to see the evolution of concepts in Uwe Rosenberg's games, especially in how Glass Road would end up borrowing the topographic elements in OeL.

No chance, tabbed resource-landscapes boards, pawns, building cards, rondel, etc.
Too many resources, I remember 14 front / back uneven, too many types of buildings, too many ways to score. Is overwhelming.
You can play on the boards outside activating a foreign laborer in building outside, which you are required to also be mindful of the boards of the other players. They can do the same to you and you often wake up to "snoop" the outside boards.
Very prone to paralysis, recommended to play which chronometer or else take a Valium.
It can be suitable for people with an IQ of 130 or higher, or for an IQ 100 with more patience than a Saint.
Is not objectively bad game, but it's not for me.

RATING: 7
[2012-02-10]
Majdnem játék nélkül vágtam bele a megvételébe, de jól döntöttem, hogy előtte csak leültem vele játszani. Sok mindent tudtam róla, hogy Le Havre, Agricola keverék ugyanazzal a szerzővel és hogy nagyon sokan dicsérik. Nos a játék tényleg nagy koppintása főleg a Le Havre-nak, de teljesen más a fő pontszerző mechanizmusa. Ugyanis itt nemcsak a minél jobb épületek beszerzése az egyik fontos feladat, hanem ezek minél jobb lehelyezése a táblánkra. Ezt még megspékeli, hogy vannak olyan falukártyák, amik adott időpontban építhetők csak és ezek csak az eddigi épületeinkből tudnak újabb pontokat kihozni a melléépítésükkel, semmi másra nem jók. A termékszerzési mechanizmus annyiban módosult, hogy egy körön vándorolnak a megszerezhető nyersanyagok, amelyekből megszerezhető mindig nő, ahogy a következő kör kezdődik, majd nullázódik, ha valaki azt termeli. Ez nagyon tetszett, ötletes, jól működő mechanizmus. Ötletes a fa és vályog megszerzésének különlegessége. Ezeket adott lapkánk eldobásával lehet szerezni. De a fő pontszerző rész nem jött be. A Le Havre-ban pont az tetszett, hogy a sok nyersanyagból, hogyan tudom a legjobb átdolgozással a legtöbb kész terméket előállítani, majd ezekből eladni és újabb épületeket venni. Itt is megvan ez a mechanizmus, de nagyon megbolondítja, pontosabban felülírja ezt az építkezés pontossága. Sokkal inkább azon van a hangsúly, hogy hogy tudja valaki a legjobb módon lehelyezni az épületeit, semmit az gazdálkodáson. Nem rossz játék az Ora, de egyrészt erős nyúlás az elődből és véleményem szerint nem is olyan jó, emiatt az épületlehelyezési fontosság miatt. Viszont rövidebb az elődnél. Én ettől függetlenül maradok a Le Havre mellett.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
1 play, 4er

Wow, great game! Feels a lot like Le Havre, which I love, but with enough differences to still be worthwhile owning both. Some differences:
- The wheel is a clever way to replace the goods system of Le Havre (although here, every good becomes more plentiful with every turn of the wheel instead of just specific goods); less fiddly, less cumbersome
- Only the start player has 2 turns per round.
- Everyone has 3 workers, 1 of which is special and may be placed on a building immediately when you build it.
- Players mostly use their own buildings
- There's a positional/spatial element, with each player expanding their own territory, creating space to build, and with building position having potentially huge effects on the player's VPs.
- Buildings come out in 4 steps, but a large number become available all at once in each step ... makes it REALLY tough to learn the game!
- No food requirement each turn; sort of -- in order to build the high-VP building (settlement) when the chance (only 5 times in game) comes around, you need to have collected enough food in the previous turns

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This game has a lot of good going for it.  I love the resource wheel, it is an innovative, fun, and easy way to handle resource collection and game management.  Absolutely brilliant!  I'm a big fan of worker placement games in general and I love some of the unique ideas in this game, such as the work contract mechanic.  I also, as weird as this sounds, just really like games where the strategy is based on turning stuff into other stuff (my friend calls it Transformer Syndrome).  Not sure why, I know it's weird, but I enjoy it and Ora et Labora is the holy grail for that mechanic.

It also helps that there appears to be multiple paths to  victory you can strive for in this game.  In my first play I only won by 12 points and my strategy was focused on buying the most valuable buildings in the game, many of which I never intended to use while the second place player focused mainly on collecting valuable resources with victory point values.  Two completely different paths to victory and a very small score margin, give or take a turn and I could have lost by a handful of points.

This also creates a game that comes with a lot of replay value out-of-box.  Not on the level of my favorite game, Agricola, but with so many paths to victory you can play a different way every time.  There's a decent variety of buildings that scales from 2-4 players and two completely different settings (France and Ireland) with different buildings and resources.

With all that I know I should love this game but it has some annoying parts that hindered my enjoyment of the game.

First, the masses are right, the quality of the components sucks.  Anybody who says different is fighting an uphill battle to defend this game which as I've said above has a lot of good going for it but the components suck.  Flimsy paper instead of cardboard for the plots is awful.  They curl in the humidity and are too flimsy to handle conveniently.  They slide around the table when building your monastic society and simply look trashy for a game with this kind of pedigree.  The cards are of equally low quality, the pegs for the wheels are not properly sized, and the chits are not properly punched.  I've had worse quality components in other great games and I don't hold it too much against the game but it is a huge disappointment, especially the paper used for the plots.  The other, even worse, gripe I have about this game is the huge amount of cleanup time.  Not only are there millions of chits to pick up and separate into containers (some sort of Plano or otherwise storage solution is REQUIRED  -- it's a grave oversight that the game does not come with some sort of solution of its own -- unless you like rifling through a ton of tiny square double-sided chits every game), but sorting all the cards (which are also double-sided for the two game settings) and plots (which need to be sorted by value) takes a long time and if you don't do it properly you just move the time taken to the next game's setup time.  But I mostly ignore this downside because for the game to exist in its form this is a necessary evil.

It also doesn't help this game's chances that I have other great, and a LOT less fiddly, games that fill the same kind of role.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

------------------------------------------------------

Now arrived and its looking good, apart from the player aids which might as well have been printed on toilet paper, instant lamination.

I have only dabbled in a solitaire 2 player run out. With the removal of feeding and addition of a VP generating phase, the spatial planning and the cool WP/Contract mechanic this could be Le Havre + - however, that is my worry. I already have found myself thinking 'Why do i need to learn a whole new set of good conversions whilst i am still happy to play Le Havre?'. The answer maybe that this is better, I suspect the first few games are going to require breaking down Le Havre hard wiring and relearning.

-----------------------------------------------------

After a three player game I can dispel Le Havre worries - this is fantastic, a synthesised and smoothed Le Havre with a splash of Farmers of the Moor. Maybe for its age its not a great, epoch making,  game, in the same way 'Gric/Le Havre dominated and defined 2007 and 2008 for me.

The possibilities in terms of balancing good, buildings and settlements seem  like they might take dozens  of plays to explore. Ownership of buildings seems less important than Le havre, and there are no 'commanding heights' the buildings feel painfully balanced with no no-brainers as to choice. The same with the goods, the importance of the basic goods through out the game feels like a real improvement on Le Havre. My one play did not explore the possibilities of blocking and Prior abuse which i hope is there. One thing I like about Le havre is the competition for the key buildings - the compensation in Ora is the variety in paths to points. 2011 has been a bloody good year for games.

--------------------------------------

Funny after 2 plays I still think this is a great game. however one that, in 2011, feels a little like a side track - its goods conversion par excellence however its does not feel as good now as Le Havre did in 2008, despite it possibly being a better game. If there were only room for this or Le Havre in my collection it would be Le Havre - I like the semi random set up and the battles for the strategic buildings that ensue. Moreover the fact there is, almost, an uninterrupted line to the final conversions means that duffers like me don't have a hope as the brightest will almost always win :)

--------------------

Very odd - lots of opportunities to play but very little desire to compared to Le Havre. 

4th game, unfortunately, confirmed my indifference. 2p it feels far less interactive than Agricola or Le Havre, very little competition for key resources or actions. The myriad paths to victory don't engage me.  This is a game I admire much more than I enjoy. 

Its weird but I prefer piles of resources - maybe just the way I think but a big pile is more attractive to take than a number on a dial

RATING: 8
The game achieves a nice stretch between a game where you build something up and always get more/have more options and a game where nevertheless you feel like you never nearly get to do enough.

3 hours for a 2-player game? Yeah,right! Maybe with 2 players that know the game by heart. All these resources/wares,all these buildings that do something different with them - this game is totally overwhelming. Especially during the first session. This is the brainburner that all others take classes at. It makes my head hurt, but it makes it hurt in a good way. 

On a side note: I think it plays quite different from Le Havre. While it shares some elements or shows the same traits concering engine-building, there's some nice touches added. Like making space for new buildings (or settlements) by cutting wood, getting turf or "exploring new areas, increased interactivity due to the commissioned works and the settlements which have a unique kind of scoring. I really wouldn't know how one could see this as less interactive than Le Havre or Agricola. 
All that makes it different enough to justify a place in my collection (of course it's fun enough for that too)

RATING: 8
I like Agricola and really like Le Havre, so it is no surprise that I have also enjoyed my first few plays of Rosenberg's latest offering in this vein. I don't have enough plays yet to fully evaluate this very complex system, but I have some early thoughts. The open system that allows you to play with various production paths to score points is much more comparable to Le Havre than to Agricola, which requires you to do very specific things (and a bit of everything).  And there does not appear to be something approaching the importance of Le Havre's coal/coke/iron/steel production chain (although something may emerge for us with more experience). 

Other thoughts: I like the variable feeding associated with village building more than the forced, set feeding amounts of the other games -- more player flexibility to play around with the system. I haven't tried France yet but am intrigued by two different sets of buildings and resources to experiment with. 

It is still too early to say, but I very well might end up liking this even more than Rosenberg's other gems.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
There are many common things with Le Havre, but worth to have both games. Personally I prefer Ora et Labora. It is a faster game, it is simpler to play, it does not have the hidden trap of feeding your people that dominates any strategy that you choose and eventually makes the game a race to build the big ships at the end. Ora et labora is like Le Havre, but it is a straight forward race to build the right buildings at the right places and generate the most points. It is a more open game with respect to what is possible to do and achieve more points than the opponents. Le Havre for players that know how to play the game is more a scripted exercise on getting/using the right buildings that will allow you to feed your people and build the big ships at the end. You cannot deviate from this path if you want to win. Ora et Labora does not have a path. It is more open and many different strategies can be pursued. You need to focus on some objective eventually, but you choose what objective and when you commit yourself in it!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In the same vein as Agricola and Le Havre.  If you love those games, which I do, this is more of the same, with just enough different to be new and interesting. I finally decided the only way I'm going to get to play is to bring my own copy.

There is a communal pool of buildings you can build.  Buildings can give you points in one of two ways, either outright, or by being next to a settlement.  Sometimes a building is better at one or the other, or even takes away points.  Buildings allow you to do special things, but your neighbors can also hire you to activate a building for them.  You have three workers, one of which can trigger a building you just built, but if he's not available, no such luck.  Resources keep accumulating every round until someone takes them.  Five times during the game you'll build a settlement, which is the main use of food and energy, which give bonus points themselves and additionally can give even more points if you put them next to some great buildings.  There are a lot of buildings that improve your resources into better food, energy, points, or just give you special goods.  

It helps in this game to figure out a chain of actions that will get you from what you have to some way to get a lot of points.  There are a ton of buildings so this may take you a while to get down.  Building placement is important and can get you a lot of points (my particular weakness with this game).  

The game has a tone of resources and buildings, so it's pretty fiddly and complicated, but it's an incredible game nonetheless.

RATING: 7
Rosenberg is hit or miss for me. After one play, this seems like a hit.  This is the Euro built out to its theoretical maximum.  There are several currencies, each of which is turned into different currencies, which are worth victory points, but can be used in other ways to earn more victory points.  Throw in a dash of building a tableau and a twist on worker placement, and this hits all the important tickboxes of Euro.  It hangs together pretty solidly.  The buildings seem appropriate, the commodities seem natural despite the huge number, and there's enough interesting thematic twists to keep it from feeling like a generic cube-pusher.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Played twice. In theory, Ora et Labora might be the most compelling of Rosenberg's games to date: the spatial element is more involved, the worker placement has an interesting added layer, and the resource wheel cleverly reduces the moving of chits around. In practice, however, I have found Ora way more difficult to get into than any of his previous efforts. 

Like Le Havre, the rules are relatively simple- the complexity of the game arises from the several dozen unique buildings and their functions. The difference is that in Le Havre the buildings are extremely intuitive, e.g. at the bakehouse you turn grain into bread, at the shipping line you ship goods, etc. In Ora, the buildings seldom make any thematic sense. A typical building in Ora lets you turn 1 sheep and 1 barley into 2 books and a statue. WTF? What's worse is that the production chains in Ora are so much more convoluted than in Le Havre (or Agricola, for that matter). 

When explaining Le Havre, I can point to a few of the pivotal buildings: wharf, brickworks, shipping line, in order to give an idea of the game's narrative arc. In Ora there is no overarching narrative (that I can tell) but rather a myriad of smaller production chains that culminate in piecemeal VP awards. The result is that playing Ora as a beginner feels largely aimless; knowing what to do presupposes an in depth understanding of all the different buildings, many of which won't even appear until the later stages of the game. 

I'm all for opaque games, but Ora isn't opaque so much as  just dense. The "depth" of Ora is more akin to the depth of ASL than to that of GO. For lovers of CCGs and other exercises in combinatorial masturbation, Ora will likely hit a sweet spot. I can tolerate this aspect in games, but it isn't the reason I play them. I've had a blast with Agricola and Le Havre, but I'm not sure whether I have it in me to get past the learning curve of Ora in order to reach the part I enjoy where the focus is on interactive game play rather than parsing the system. Maybe I'm just irrevocably burned out on this type of game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

BGG Top 100
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I think I am comfortable with saying this is probably Uwe's best effort so far. It does a nice job of balancing predictability with lots of options to keep each playthrough feeling fresh. It's the usual soup of course, gathering resources with workers, then converting them into better resources and eventually cashing them out as points. While no new ground is really turned here, what has been added is spatial planning. Unlike previous Uwe games, where you build each new building in relation to your other buildings matters. Uwe first started toying with this in the Farmers of the Moor expansion for Agricola and it really blossoms here.It's a small thing, but it adds so much to the game.

There are also two different scenarios in the box, one focusing on French monasteries and one focusing on the Irish. It's a fun little touch, with the two having slightly different tech trees and resources. It also screams for expansion, so hopefully, they get a little expansion happy like they did with Agricola.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
Revisiting this after a few years away. The spatial aspect could be more interesting: add a little pickup/deliver aspect, or something beyond this minimalist jigsaw, but it can create tricky priorities to juggle.

The lack of a clear strategic line makes it more than just Le Havre 2.0; the original seems better on most counts, but I'm burned on coal/coke/shipping. Really wish there was a fixed endgame for 2p, as the need to count out the scores and the inputs/outputs is tedious. Just a really strange and unnecessary choice.

Clearly this relies on the butterfly effect for variation, and it mostly succeeds (Uwe's puzzles are fascinating), but another card set would be very welcome. Or one where you flip half of them over at random, etc. So I made one: [filepage=148632][/filepage].

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
[i]I'm bumping the rating from 7 to 8 (details below).[/i]

It's a good, well-designed game - no doubt about that. I love many aspects of it, but...

I appreciate what Uwe tried to do here (it's a very clever resource conversion/spatial puzzle), but it is just too long and overwhelming. You have to memorize the whole production chain and know the future decks inside out to play efficiently and have a long-term plan of any sort. Also, at some point the game gets out of control, there's too many action spaces and possibilities -  planning your strategy around that is extremely hard. 

Still, I see a lot of potential in this game. Maybe it gets less overwhelming after repeated plays. I definitely need to play it again (maybe I'll even grab myself a copy and do some extensive testing).

/update: Bought my own copy. I kept thinking about this game and this is very unusual for me. There's definitely something about it that makes me want to play it more. 

//another update: I played it again, this time with 4 players. It's even more overwhelming that way, but... something just clicked. I don't know why, but I started to really enjoy the thought process behind every decision made in this game. I'm really glad I gave it another shot, there's a whole plethora of strategies only waiting to be discovered. Usually I don't like sandbox games, but Ora et Labora has that special something. It's my second favourite Uwe Rosenberg's design right now (after Agricola).

I like the multiplayer as your choices are far more limited and other player actions affect what you are able to do on your turn (and vice versa), but the solo game is not for me.

RATING: 6
One play of the France version and one with Ireland.  Feels like Le Havre with the clunky parts (like feeding every turn) thrown out, and a wider array of choices.  I like it better than Le Havre.  

Components are a little annoying in that it's quite difficult to make out opponents' buildings from across the table (with 20/20 vision).  I did like the rondel as a solution to tracking the number of the various resources that were available.  And the component quality other than the cards was great.

I think that this game is an excellent representative of this style of game, and other than the components, I don't see how I would make it better.  That said, I don't think that this style of "get basic resources which you turn into this to turn into that to get victory points" is really my kind of game.  Probably too much cold calculation without enough randomness to keep the AP at bay.

RATING: 3
I'm a bit underwhelmed with games such as Ora et Labora, and its older sibling Le Havre, where all your options feel like good choices. There's no stress, nor fretting over difficult decisions. Instead everyone has a multitude of great options available and the game feels a bit too light-hearted to me. You just seem to stumble over points in Ora et Labora, and never feel stressed about obtaining the food and little energy needed for your next settlement. I prefer the stress of the Harvest phase in Agricola any day!

Ora et Labora should nonetheless see frequent play if I know my gaming buddies well enough, but when it's my turn to pick I will rather play something else. That said, I look forward to another 3-4 plays before cementing my final opinion.
